Next April?" said another.[\[1\]](#page-209-4) Most of us who lived through the '80s remember what happened next; the *Challenger* exploded seventy-three seconds after taking off. The criteria for the launch date decision should obviously have been focused on promoting the goal of safety, not speed.

> 然而，在发射前几天的一次飞行前会议上，挑战者号项目的承包商 Morton Thiokol 的工程师们情绪激动，甚至流下了眼泪。他们知道，发射当天预测的气温可能太低，导致航天飞机的O型环无法正常工作。如果O型环失效，后果将非常严重。他们希望有时间解决问题，或等待温度更高的发射日期，并恳请 NASA 延迟发射。但他们的请求被拒绝了。一位 NASA 官员表示：“我对你们的建议感到震惊。”另一位则说：“你们什么时候想让我发射？下个月四月？”[1] (#page-209-4) 我们这代人中大多数都记得接下来发生了什么；挑战者号在升空后73秒爆炸了。显然，决定发射日期的标准应该以促进安全目标为核心，而不是追求速度。

<span id="page-129-1"></span><span id="page-129-0"></span>The inertia default can also inspire us to adopt criteria that aren't goal promoting. For example, upper management might fail to see that market conditions have changed. Rather than taking the time to understand the new conditions and adjust their criteria accordingly, they continue using the criteria they've used in the past even though those criteria aren't goal promoting in the present.

> 惯性思维也可能促使我们采用那些不再有助于实现目标的标准。例如，高层管理者可能未能意识到市场环境已经发生了变化。他们没有花时间去理解新的环境并据此调整标准，而是继续沿用过去的标准，即使这些标准在当前环境下已经不再有助于实现目标。

Criteria can also fail to be decisive. If they don't help you narrow the options, they're not useful. Indecisive criteria are another sign that you don't fully understand the problem and are operating out of fear that you'll be wrong. The social default preys on people who don't want to take responsibility for outcomes or who don't have clear ideas of what they want.

> 标准也可能缺乏决定性。如果它们无法帮助你缩小选择范围，那它们就没有用。缺乏决断力的标准是另一个迹象，表明你并未完全理解问题，而是在出于害怕犯错的心态下进行决策。社会默认做法往往针对那些不愿为结果承担责任，或对自身真正想要什么缺乏清晰认知的人。

Think of choosing a restaurant for dinner with a group of friends. Someone will make an initial suggestion—like eating Mexican—and inevitably someone else will say, "I just had Mexican last night." Then you'll hear, "What about salads?" and someone will say, "I'm too hungry for salad." On and on ad nauseam: people say what they don't want until the group is so hungry they pick whatever is most convenient. I've seen this situation play out the same way so often it's comical. (Pay attention the next time it's happening to you!)

> 想象一下，和一群朋友一起选晚餐餐厅的情景。有人会先提出一个建议——比如吃墨西哥菜——但很快就会有另一个人说：“我昨晚刚吃过墨西哥菜。”接着又会有人提议：“那要不我们吃沙拉？”又有人会说：“我现在太饿，吃不下去沙拉。”如此反复，直到大家饿得受不了，只能随便选一个最方便的选项。我见过太多次这样的场景，简直滑稽可笑。（下次再遇到这种情况时，可要留心观察哦！）

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> “ad nauseam” 是拉丁语，意为“令人厌烦地”，这里用来形容反复无尽的讨论，翻译时用“如此反复”来传达原意。

The problem here is that in many cases, purely negative criteria aren't decisive: they don't narrow the field of options down to one. As a result, people end up leaving the ultimate choice up to chance or circumstance. As the old saying goes, "If you don't know where you want to go, any road will take you there."

> 这里的问题在于，很多时候纯粹的负面标准并不能决定一切：它们无法将选择的范围缩小到一个选项。因此，人们最终往往将最终的选择交给运气或环境。正如一句古老的谚语所说：“如果你不知道自己想要去哪里，那么任何一条路都会带你到达那里。”

<span id="page-130-0"></span>Suppose, by contrast, that when you and your friends were deciding where to eat, each of you stated not what you didn't want but rather what you did:

> 相比之下，假设当你和朋友们决定去哪家吃饭时，每个人都没有说出自己不想吃什么，而是说出自己想吃什么。

- "I want somewhere that serves salads within ten minutes' walking distance."
- "I want somewhere that serves burgers."
- "I really don't care; I just want to eat soon."

> 我想找一个在步行十分钟内就能到达的地方，那里有沙拉供应。
我想找一个有汉堡供应的地方。
我真的不在乎，我只想尽快吃点东西。

Making the decision would be much faster, and it would be more likely to get more people what they wanted.

> 做出决定会更快，也更有可能让更多人得到他们想要的东西。

#### 0.1.1 **Defining the Most Important Thing**

> 29.1.1.1 确定最重要的事情

Not all criteria are the same. There might be a hundred variables, but they are not equally important. When you're clear on what's important, evaluating options becomes easier. Many people are shy to pick out the most important thing because they don't want to be wrong.

> 并非所有标准都同等重要。可能会有上百个变量，但它们的重要性各不相同。当你清楚什么最重要时，评估选项就会变得更容易。许多人之所以不敢挑出最关键的因素，是因为他们不想犯错。

When you don't communicate what's most important, people are left guessing about what matters. They need you to solve the problem for them. While you feel needed and important, you're also busy making all the decisions that your colleagues should be making.

> 当你没有沟通最重要的事情时，人们就会对什么真正重要感到困惑。他们需要你为他们解决问题。虽然你觉得自己被需要且重要，但你却忙于做本应由同事来做的一切决定。

A lot of managers secretly enjoy being the bottleneck. They like the way it feels when their team is dependent on them. Don't be fooled! This is the ego default at work, and it puts a ceiling on how far you will go. It tries to convince you that you're the best; that you're so smart, so skilled, so insightful that only you can make the decisions. In reality, you're just getting in the way of the team performing at its best.

> 很多经理私下里其实享受成为瓶颈的感觉。他们喜欢自己团队依赖自己的感觉。别被蒙蔽了！这就是自恋心理在作祟，它会限制你前进的上限。它试图说服你，你是最棒的；你那么聪明、那么有技能、那么有洞察力，只有你才能做决定。实际上，你只是在阻碍团队发挥出最佳水平。

I learned this lesson the hard way. I had just taken over a team and was surprised that they would check in with me before making any decisions—a pattern their previous manager had established.

> 我通过亲身经历才明白这个道理。我刚接手一个团队，没想到他们会在做任何决定前先向我汇报——这是他们前一位经理留下的习惯。

To speed things up, I came up with a system for them to sort decisions into three boxes:

> 为了加快进度，我为他们设计了一个系统，将决策分为三个盒子。

- 1. decisions they could make without any input from me,
- 2. decisions they could make after sharing their reasoning with me so I could double-check their judgment, and
- 3. decisions I wanted to make myself.

> 他们可以在我完全不参与的情况下做出的决定，  
他们可以与我分享他们的理由后做出的决定，这样我可以再检查一遍他们的判断，以及  
我想自己做出的决定。

### 0.2 But the problem persisted.

> 29.1.2 但问题依然存在。

After a few months, I consulted my mentor. "Do they know what decisions they should make and what decisions you want to make?" he asked. "Are the boxes clear?"

> 几个月后，我向我的导师请教。“他们知道应该做出什么决定，以及你希望做出什么决定吗？”他问道。“目标是否清晰？”

"Yes," I replied, "but due to the operational nature of our job, if I'm not around, they have to make decisions in the third box without me. That's where we're running into the biggest problems. They seem incapable of doing that."

> “是的，”我回答道，“但因为我们的工作具有操作性质，如果我不在场，他们就必须在我缺席的情况下在第三个箱子里做出决策。正是在这个环节，我们遇到了最大的问题。他们似乎完全不具备这样做能力。”

"Do they know the one thing that's most important?" he probed.

> 他问道：“他们知道最重要的一件事吗？”

"I'm not sure what you mean," I said. "What's most important differs for each decision." I listed off a few different types of decisions and how the variables were different.

> “我不确定你指的是什么，”我说，“对于每个决定来说，最重要的东西各不相同。”我列举了几种不同类型的决定以及它们所涉及的变量如何不同。

"That's not what I mean," he replied. "Do they know what you value most?" I hesitated. He looked me square in the eye. "Shane, do *you* know what you value most?" I stared at him blankly. He sighed. "The problem isn't your team. It's you. You don't know what's most important. Until you do, your team will never make decisions without you. It's too risky for them to figure out the most important thing. Communicate that to your team, and they'll be able to make decisions on their own."

> “这并不是我的意思，”他回答道。“他们知道你最看重什么吗？”我犹豫了一下。他直视着我的眼睛。“肖恩，你知道你最看重什么吗？”我茫然地盯着他。他叹了口气。“问题不在于你的团队，而在于你自己。你并不知道自己最看重什么。直到你明白这一点，你的团队才不会在没有你的情况下做决定。对他们来说，要自己判断出最重要的事情风险太高了。把这一点传达给你的团队，他们就能自己做决定。”

"What if they make the wrong decision?"

> 如果他们做出错误的决定怎么办？

<span id="page-131-0"></span>"As long as they make a decision based on the most important thing, they won't be wrong." He paused, then said slowly, "A lot of people reach their ceiling in this job because they can't figure out this one thing."

> 只要他们基于最重要的事情做出决定，就不会犯错。”他停顿了一下，然后慢慢说道：“很多人在这个工作中达到瓶颈，是因为他们无法弄清楚这一点。”

I learned three important lessons that day. First, I couldn't expect my team to make decisions on their own unless I told them how I wanted them to make those decisions. That meant focusing on the single most important thing and not inundating them with hundreds of variables to consider. Second, if they made the decision with the most important thing in mind, and it turned out wrong, I couldn't get upset with them. If I did that, they'd never make decisions without me. The third lesson was perhaps the most revealing: I myself didn't know what the most important thing was. That's why I couldn't tell them.

> 那天我学到了三个重要的教训。第一，除非我告诉他们我想要他们如何做决定，否则我不能指望他们自己做决定。这意味着要专注于最重要的事情，而不是给他们灌输数百个需要考虑的变量。第二，如果他们以最重要的事情为出发点做出决定，结果却错了，我不能因此而责怪他们。如果我这么做，他们以后就永远不会在没有我参与的情况下做决定了。第三点可能是最发人深省的：我自己都不清楚最重要的事情到底是什么。这也就是为什么我无法告诉他们。

#### 0.2.1 **How to Safeguard the Evaluation Stage**

> 29.1.2.1 如何保障评估阶段

There is only one most important thing in every project, goal, and company. If you have two or more most important things, you're not thinking clearly. This is an important aspect of leadership and problem-solving in general: you have to pick one criterion above all the others and communicate it in a way that your people can understand so they can make decisions on their own. This is true leadership. You need to be clear about what values people are to use when making decisions. If I tell you the most important thing is serving the customer, you know how to make decisions without me. If you make a bad judgment call, but it puts the customer first, I can't fault you. You did what I wanted.

> 每个项目、目标和公司中，只有一件最重要之事。如果你有两个或更多最重要的事情，那就说明你没有清晰地思考。这是领导力和解决问题的一个重要方面：你必须在所有其他标准之上选择一个标准，并以人们能够理解的方式传达出来，使他们能够自主做出决策。这才是真正的领导力。你必须清楚地告诉人们在做决策时应遵循哪些价值观。如果我告诉你最重要的事情是服务客户，那么你就能在不需要我指导的情况下做出决策。即使你做出的判断有误，但只要把客户放在首位，我就不责怪你。你已经做到了我想要的。

But identifying what's most important is a skill. It takes practice. Here's how.

> 但确定什么是最重要的是一项技能。它需要练习。以下是方法。

<span id="page-132-0"></span>I recommend using sticky notes for this exercise. First, on each sticky note, write out one criterion—one thing that's important to you—in evaluating your options.

> 我建议你使用便签纸来完成这个练习。首先，在每张便签纸上写下一条标准——也就是对你来说重要的一个因素——用来评估你的选择。

For example, before I decided to invest in Pixel Union—one of the largest and best design agencies in the Shopify universe—I wrote down some criteria that were important to me.

> 例如，在我决定投资Pixel Union——这家Shopify宇宙中规模最大、最优秀的平面设计公司之一——之前，我写下了一些对我而言很重要的标准。

They included:

> 他们包括：

- A win-win for employees, customers, and shareholders
- Growing rather than shrinking the business
- Working with people I trust
- Not having to manage people or add more to my plate
- Not borrowing money
- A high probability of a decent return on investment

> 员工、客户和股东皆获益
让公司持续增长而非萎缩
与我信赖的人共事
无需管理他人或增加额外负担
不借贷
有较高概率获得合理的投资回报

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> "A win-win for employees, customers, and shareholders" 翻译为“员工、客户和股东皆获益”，意为各方都能从中获益，达到双赢局面。

There are many more, but you get the idea. Place only one criterion on each sticky note because next, we're going to make your criteria battle.

> 还有很多，但你已经明白我的意思了。每个便利贴上只写一个标准，因为接下来我们要进行标准之间的“战斗”。

Choose whichever criterion you think is the most important to you and place it on the wall. Then grab another criterion. Compare each and ask, "If I absolutely had to choose between only these two, which matters more?"

> 选择对你而言最重要的一个标准，把它贴在墙上。然后拿另一个标准，将它们逐一进行比较，并问自己：“如果我只能在这两个之间做出选择，哪一个更为重要？”

So to return to my example of investing in Pixel Union, the first battle might be this: earning a return on investment versus not having to manage people or add more to my plate.

> 因此，回到我之前举的例子，即投资Pixel Union，第一场战斗可能是这样的：获得投资回报，还是不必管理他人或增加自己的负担。

If I could have only one of these—if either earning a return on my investment required managing people, or not managing people entailed making less money—which would it be? I'd choose earning more money even if it involved managing people. So I'd move that criterion higher.

> 如果我只能在这两种情况中选择一种——如果要获得投资回报就必须管理他人，或者不管理他人就意味着赚不到更多的钱——我会选哪一种？我会选择赚更多钱，即使这意味着要管理他人。因此，我会把这条标准提得更高一些。

Of course, I'd be willing to manage people only up to a point. If doing that became too time-consuming, I might have to reverse the order. That leads us to the next step: adding quantities. As your criteria battle one another, you'll find quantities make a difference. Add them to each criterion as they battle.

> 当然，我只愿意在一定范围内管理他人。如果这样做变得太过耗时，我可能不得不反过来处理。这引出了下一步：添加权重。当你的标准彼此竞争时，你会发现权重会产生差异。在它们相互竞争时，为每个标准添加相应的权重。

<span id="page-133-0"></span>Suppose I find that I'm willing to spend five to ten additional hours per week managing people or being hands on, so long as my ROI is at least 15 percent per year. If I'd have to invest ten-plus hours per week, my ROI would have to be at least 20 percent per year, and if I'd have to invest twenty-plus hours per week, it would no longer be worth it to me—regardless of anticipated return because of the opportunity cost of that time.

> 假设我发现自己愿意每周多花五到十个小时来管理他人或亲自动手，前提是我的投资回报率（ROI）至少达到每年15%。如果我必须每周投入超过十个小时，那么我的ROI就需要达到至少每年20%。如果我必须每周投入超过二十个小时，那么无论预期回报如何，对我而言这都不再值得，因为那段时间的机会成本太高了。

When you've finished ordering these two criteria, go to the next pair. Move from top to bottom, make your criteria battle one another for priority, and add the quantities that matter to you along the way.

> 当你完成对这两个标准的排序后，进入下一组标准。从上到下依次进行，让这些标准彼此争夺优先级，并在过程中添加对你而言重要的量化指标。

When people do this exercise, they often look at a pair of criteria and think, "I don't necessarily have to choose between those two." Make them battle anyway! The point isn't really to compare them, it's to find out which one is more important. Maybe in real life you can satisfy both criteria—maybe, for instance, you can get a high ROI while investing in a socially responsible company, or you can get in shape while still eating out three times a week, or you can buy a house in a great location that fits your budget. But often, when we actually start pursuing an option, we find that we have to rank one criterion above another—even if only slightly. Most of the time, making your criteria battle is about calibrating shades of gray. It's a mental exercise that takes you out of reactive mode and moves you toward deliberative thinking.

> 当人们做这个练习时，往往会面对一对标准，然后想：“我并不一定非要在这两者之间做出选择。” 让它们彼此争斗吧！这并不是真的要比较它们，而是为了弄清楚哪一个更重要。也许在现实生活中，你确实可以同时满足这两个标准——比如，你可以在投资时获得高回报，同时又选择一家具有社会责任感的公司；或者，你可以在保持身材的同时，每周仍外出就餐三次；又或者，你可以在预算范围内买到一个地理位置优越的房子。但通常，当我们真正开始追求某个选择时，就会发现必须将其中一个标准置于另一个之上——哪怕只是稍微地。大多数情况下，让标准彼此争斗，其实是为了校准那些灰色地带。这是一种心理练习，它能让你摆脱反应式的思维模式，转向更加审慎的思考。

<span id="page-134-0"></span>Assigning quantitative values to your criteria often helps at this point. When you start comparing things and thinking how much you'll pay for them—and whether in a currency of time, money, collective brain power—you gain clarity about what matters most to you and what doesn't. You're forced to think in terms of benefits and risks, and you start to see things you didn't see before previously invisible costs become visible. For all of these reasons, making your criteria battle moves you toward objectivity and accuracy, and helps reveal what you think is most important.

> 给你的标准赋予量化值通常在这个阶段会很有帮助。当你开始比较事物，并思考你要为它们付出多少——无论是时间、金钱还是集体智慧——你就会更清楚什么对你来说最重要，什么并不重要。你被迫以利益和风险的角度进行思考，也开始看到以前未曾注意到的东西，那些之前隐形的成本变得清晰可见。出于所有这些原因，明确你的标准会使你更接近客观和准确，从而揭示出你真正认为最重要的东西。

>[!note]- 翻译注释  
> "battle moves" 翻译为“明确你的标准”是根据上下文意译，意指通过设定标准来推进决策过程。

Once you've settled on your criteria and their order of importance, it's time to apply them to the options. Doing so requires that you have information about those options that meets two conditions: it's *relevant*, and it's *accurate*.

> 在确定了您的标准及其重要性顺序后，下一步就是将这些标准应用到各个选项上。要做到这一点，您需要有关这些选项的信息满足两个条件：信息必须相关，而且必须准确。

#### 0.2.2 **Most Information Is Irrelevant**
> 29.1.2.2 大多数信息都是无关的

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> 该标题中的“Most Information Is Irrelevant”翻译为“大多数信息都是无关的”，意指在大量信息中，真正相关的内容并不多。

When it comes to getting information that's relevant to the decision, remember this:

> 在获取与决策相关的信息时，请记住这一点：

**the targeting principle:** Know what you're looking for before you start sorting through the data.

> 目标原则：在开始筛选数据之前，先明确你要找什么。

If you don't know what you're looking for, you're unlikely to find it, just as you're unlikely to hit the target if you don't know what you're aiming at. When you don't know what's important, you miss things that are relevant and spend a lot of time on things that are irrelevant.

> 如果你不知道自己在找什么，就不太可能找到它，就像你不知道自己要瞄准什么，就不太可能击中目标一样。当你不知道什么是重要的，就会错过相关的事物，却花大量时间在无关紧要的事情上。

Most information is irrelevant. Knowing what to ignore separating the signal from the noise—is the key to not wasting valuable time. Think, for example, of investment decisions. The best investors know which variables probabilistically govern the outcomes, and they pay attention to those. They don't ignore everything else, but focusing primarily on those variables allows them to filter massive amounts of information very quickly.

> 大多数信息都是无关紧要的。知道该忽略什么，从而将信号从噪音中区分开来，是避免浪费宝贵时间的关键。例如，想想投资决策。最好的投资者清楚哪些变量在概率上决定了结果，并且他们会关注这些变量。他们并非忽视其他一切，但主要关注这些变量使他们能够迅速过滤大量信息。

<span id="page-135-0"></span>People who can quickly distinguish what matters from what doesn't gain a huge advantage in a world where the flow of information never stops.

> 那些能够迅速区分什么是重要的、什么是不重要的人们，在这个信息不断流动的世界中获得了巨大的优势。

Knowing what to ignore allows you to focus on what matters. Follow the example of the best investors and know the variables that matter for evaluating the options *before* you start sorting through information.

> 知道该忽略什么，才能专注于真正重要的事情。效仿最优秀的投资者，先明确在评估选项时哪些变量最为关键，然后再开始筛选信息。

#### 0.2.3 **Getting Accurate Information from the Source**

> 29.1.2.3 从源头获取准确信息

When it comes to getting information that's accurate, there are two principles you should know: the HiFi Principle and the HiEx Principle. The first will help you find the best intel possible from within any given situation, and the second will help you find the best intel possible from outside of it.

> 获取准确信息时，有两项原则需要了解：HiFi原则和HiEx原则。前者能帮助你在任何特定情境中找到最佳情报，而后者则能帮助你从情境之外找到最佳情报。

**the hifi principle:** Get high-fidelity (HiFi) information—information that's close to the source and unfiltered by other people's biases and interests.

> HiFi原则：获取高保真（HiFi）信息——即接近信息源头、未被他人偏见和利益过滤的信息。

The quality of your decisions is directly related to the quality of your thoughts. The quality of your thoughts is directly related to the quality of your information.

> 你的决定质量直接取决于你的思维质量，而你的思维质量又直接取决于你所掌握的信息质量。

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> 该句强调思维、信息与决策之间的因果关系，翻译时保留了这种逻辑递进关系，使中文表达更自然流畅。

Many people treat all sources of information as if they're equally valid. They're not. While you might value getting everyone's opinion, that doesn't mean each opinion should be equally weighted or considered.

> 许多人将所有信息来源都视为同等有效，但实际上并非如此。虽然你可能重视收集每个人的意见，但这并不意味着每种意见都应被同等重视或对待。

A lot of the information we consume is in the form of highlights, summaries, or distillations. It's the illusion of knowledge. We learn the answer but can't show our work.

> 我们所消费的大量信息都以要点、摘要或提炼的形式呈现。这是一种知识的幻觉。我们学会了答案，却无法展示出思考过程。

Consider what happens when you consult a nutritionist. They take their years of experience and knowledge and compress it into a <span id="page-136-0"></span>list of foods to eat and behaviors to implement. If you just want the answer, they will tell you what to eat and how much. This is an abstraction; it's like you're back in sixth-grade math class, copying answers from the person beside you. Sure, you got the right answer, but you don't know why it's the answer. You lack understanding, and information without understanding is dangerous.

> 考虑一下你咨询营养师会发生什么。他们会把多年的经验和知识压缩成一份应该吃的食物清单和需要实施的行为建议。如果你只想知道答案，他们会告诉你该吃什么以及吃多少。这是一种抽象化的做法；就像你回到六年级的数学课上，抄袭旁边同学的答案。虽然你得到了正确的答案，但你并不知道为什么是这个答案。你缺乏理解，而没有理解的信息是危险的。

>[!note]- 翻译注释  
> “sixth-grade math class” 翻译为“六年级的数学课”是根据中文教育阶段的常见表达方式。

It's natural to think these abstractions will save us time and improve our decision-making, but in many cases they don't. Reading a summary might be faster than reading a full document, but it misses a lot of details—details that weren't relevant to the person summarizing the information, but that might be relevant to you. You end up saving time at the cost of missing important information. Skimming inadvertently creates blind spots.

> 人们自然会认为这些抽象概念能节省时间并提高我们的决策质量，但很多时候并非如此。阅读摘要可能比阅读完整文档更快，但它却遗漏了许多细节——这些细节对摘要信息的人来说可能并不重要，但对你而言却可能至关重要。你最终是以牺牲重要信息为代价来节省时间。略读无意间制造了认知盲点。

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> "Skimming inadvertently creates blind spots" 翻译为“略读无意间制造了认知盲点”，意指在快速浏览时，可能会忽略某些关键信息，从而形成认知上的盲区。

Information is food for the mind. What you put in today shapes your solutions tomorrow. And just as you are responsible for the food that goes into your mouth, you are responsible for the information that goes into your mind. You can't be healthy if you feed yourself junk food every day, and you can't make good decisions if you're consuming low-quality information. Higher quality inputs lead to higher quality outputs.

> 信息是大脑的营养。你今天输入的东西，将影响你明天的解决方案。就像你对自己的饮食负有责任一样，你也必须对自己的思想负有责任。如果你每天都吃垃圾食品，就不可能健康；同样，如果你长期接受低质量的信息，就无法做出明智的决策。高质量的输入才能带来高质量的输出。

>[!note]- 翻译注释  
> “Information is food for the mind” 译为“信息是大脑的营养”，既保留了原文的比喻，又符合中文表达习惯。  
> “Higher quality inputs lead to higher quality outputs” 译为“高质量的输入才能带来高质量的输出”，在中文中更自然地表达了因果关系。

The desire for abstractions is understandable. The amount of information that bombards us daily can feel overwhelming. But the further the information is from the original source, the more filters it's been through before getting to you. Living on a diet of abstractions is like living on a diet of junk food: it has less nutritional value—less information content, which means you're not learning as much.

> 对抽象概念的渴望是可以理解的。我们每天所接触到的信息量巨大，常常让人感到不知所措。但信息离其原始来源越远，它在到达你之前就经过了越多的过滤。以抽象概念为食，就像以垃圾食品为食一样：它们的营养价值更低——信息含量更少，这意味着你学到的东西也更少。

>[!note]- 翻译注释  
> “Living on a diet of junk food” 这一比喻在中文中没有直接对应的表达，因此采用“以垃圾食品为食”来传达原意。

<span id="page-136-1"></span>Real knowledge is earned, while abstractions are merely borrowed. Too often decision-makers get their information and observations from sources that are multiple degrees removed from the problem. Relying on these abstractions is a prime opportunity for the ego default to work its mischief. It conjures the illusion of knowledge: we feel confident about what to do without really understanding the problem.

> 真正的知识是通过实践获得的，而抽象概念只是借来的。决策者们常常从与问题相隔多层的来源获取信息和观察结果。依赖这些抽象概念，是自我中心倾向发挥破坏作用的绝佳机会。它会营造出一种知识的幻觉：我们对自己该做什么感到自信，却并不真正理解问题本身。

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> "ego default" 翻译为“自我中心倾向”以传达原文中“自我中心的默认状态”这一心理机制。

You can't make good decisions with bad information. In fact, when you see people making decisions that don't make sense to you, chances are they're based on different information than you've consumed. Just as junk food eventually makes you unhealthy, bad inputs eventually produce bad decisions.

> 你无法在信息不准确的情况下做出明智的决定。事实上，当你看到别人做出让你觉得不合逻辑的决定时，很可能他们所依据的信息与你所接触的信息不同。就像垃圾食品最终会让你变得不健康一样，错误的信息最终也会导致糟糕的决定。

How do we get better information?

> 我们如何获取更准确的信息？

The person closest to the problem often has the most accurate information about it. What they tend to lack is a broader perspective. The person working on the line at McDonald's knows how to fix a recurring problem at their restaurant better than a person merely analyzing some data. What they don't know is how it fits into the bigger picture. They don't know whether the problem exists everywhere, or whether the solution would cause more harm than good if implemented globally, or how to roll the idea out to everyone.

> 最了解问题的人往往也掌握着最准确的信息。但他们通常缺乏的是更广阔的视角。在麦当劳一线工作的员工比仅仅分析一些数据的人更清楚如何解决餐厅内反复出现的问题。但他们并不知道这个问题在整个大局中的位置。他们不知道这个问题是否普遍存在，或者如果在全球范围内实施解决方案是否会带来更多的负面影响，也不知道如何将这个想法推广到每一个人身上。

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> "roll the idea out to everyone" 翻译为“将这个想法推广到每一个人身上”，意指将解决方案或理念普及到所有相关人员或群体中。

<span id="page-137-0"></span>My friend Tim Urban has a good metaphor to explain this concept. In the restaurant business, there are chefs and there are line cooks.[\[2\]](#page-209-5) Both can follow a recipe. When things go according to plan, there is no difference in the process or the result. But when things go wrong, the chef knows why. The line cook often does not. The chef has cultivated depth of understanding through years of experience, experimentation, and reflection, and as a result, the chef, rather than the line cook, can diagnose problems when they arise.[\[\\*\]](#page-281-0)

> 我的朋友蒂姆·厄兰（Tim Urban）有一个很好的比喻来解释这个概念。在餐饮业中，有主厨，也有打荷厨师。[[2]](#page-209-5) 两者都可以按照食谱操作。当一切按计划进行时，过程和结果没有区别。但一旦出现意外，主厨知道问题出在哪里，而打荷厨师往往不知道。主厨通过多年的经验、实验和反思，培养出了深入的理解，因此，当问题出现时，是主厨而不是打荷厨师能够准确诊断问题。[[*]](#page-281-0)

<span id="page-137-2"></span><span id="page-137-1"></span>History shows that the greatest thinkers all used information that they collected personally. They earned their knowledge the hard way either in the trenches of experience or through careful study of exemplars. They looked for raw, unfiltered information, and ventured out into the world to interact with it directly.

> 历史表明，最伟大的思想家们都依靠自己收集的信息。他们要么通过经验的第一线战场，要么通过对典范的仔细研究，以艰苦的方式获得知识。他们追求原始、未经过滤的信息，并走出世界，直接与之互动。

Leonardo da Vinci is a great example. He kept journals throughout his life, and they contain notes about how he went about getting the right information. He wrote things like, "Get the master of arithmetic to show you how to square a triangle," and "Get a master of hydraulics to tell you how to repair a lock, canal and mill in the Lombard manner."

> 达芬奇是一个非常好的例子。他一生中都保持着笔记的习惯，这些笔记记录了他获取正确信息的方法。他写道：“请算术大师教你如何计算三角形的平方”，“请水力学大师告诉你如何按照伦巴第的方式修理水闸、运河和水磨”。

Great thinkers understand the importance of high-quality information, and that other people's abstractions are often limited in their usefulness.

> 伟大的思想家们深知高质量信息的重要性，并意识到他人的抽象概念往往在实用性上存在局限。

As information travels up an organization, it tends to lose quality and nuance. Remember that children's game, telephone: you whisper a sentence to the next person, and that person whispers it to the next, and after it passes through half the class, the message is nothing like the original sentence. No single person necessarily changes it much, but the more people it passes through, the more all those little changes accumulate. The same thing happens when information travels through an organization. It goes through multiple filters, including individual levels of understanding, political interpretation, and biases. Details are abstracted from the original, and the signal is lost. The various incentives people have when they communicate information end up complicating things even further.

> 当信息在组织中向上传递时，往往会失去其质量和细微差别。记得小时候玩过的“电话游戏”吗？你小声告诉下一个人一句话，然后那个人再小声告诉下一个人，等到信息传到一半班级时，已经和最初的内容大相径庭了。通常每个人对信息的改动并不大，但经过的人越多，那些微小的改动就会不断累积。同样的情况也会发生在信息在组织中传递时。信息会经过多个过滤环节，包括个人的理解层次、政治性解读和偏见。原始信息中的细节被抽象掉了，关键信息也随之丢失。人们在传递信息时所拥有的各种动机，还会进一步使情况变得更加复杂。

The problem isn't merely that people are unreliable transmitters of information; the problem is also that there are limitations on the information that abstractions can represent. Think of a road map. It's an abstract representation of a real landscape. That landscape includes rocks, plants, animals, cities, wind, and weather, along with many other things. We don't represent all of them when we map the landscape, only those things that interest us—for example, roads, rivers, and geographical boundaries. We pull these features away from the original and represent them in a way that makes them stand out. (That's in fact what the word "abstract" means: "to pull away from.")

> 问题不仅仅在于人们不是可靠的信息传递者；问题还在于抽象概念在表达信息时存在局限性。想象一下一张道路地图。它是一种对真实地貌的抽象表现。真实地貌包括岩石、植物、动物、城市、风、天气，以及许多其他事物。但在绘制地图时，我们并不表达所有这些内容，只表达那些对我们有意义的事物，比如道路、河流和地理边界。我们将这些特征从原本的环境中剥离出来，并以一种使其突出的方式加以表现。（事实上，“抽象”这个词的含义就是“剥离”）

Removing what doesn't serve our interests is what makes a map useful. But somewhere in the process, someone has decided what's useful and what isn't based on what interests them. What if we're interested in something else? What if we're interested in population densities or geological strata? A road map isn't designed to highlight these concepts, so it's not going to be very useful to us.

> 去除那些对我们无益的内容，才是使地图有用的要素。但在这一过程中，有人已经根据自己的兴趣，决定什么是有用的、什么是没用的。如果我们感兴趣的是其他东西呢？比如人口密度或地质层？道路地图并不是为突出这些概念而设计的，因此对我们来说，它就不会太有用了。

What's true of maps is true of any other abstractions: by nature, they're designed to serve the interests of their designers. If those designers don't have the same interests as you, their abstractions aren't going to give you the information you need. Similarly, any information you may get from a secondhand source has likely been filtered through that source's interests. Since your interests are likely different from theirs, their summaries, highlights, and descriptions are likely to leave out relevant information that could help you with your decision.

> 地图的情况也适用于其他所有抽象概念：从本质上说，它们的设计目的是为了满足设计者自身的利益。如果这些设计者与你的利益不一致，他们的抽象概念就无法为你提供你需要的信息。同样地，你从二手来源获得的任何信息，很可能已经经过了该来源利益的过滤。由于你的利益很可能与他们的不同，他们的摘要、重点和描述很可能会遗漏一些对你做决定有帮助的相关信息。

I learned the importance of accurate information while working for the CEO of a large company. Nothing crossed his desk without going through me first. Early one morning I saw an email from one of his direct reports flagging a technical problem that was affecting operations. After I told him what I'd heard about the problem, he asked a simple question: "Where did you get this information?" I replied that I'd heard it from the VP in charge of the division. The look on his face instantly turned to disappointment. Moments passed in silence.

> 在我为一家大公司的首席执行官工作时，我学会了准确信息的重要性。他办公桌上的一切文件都必须经过我才能传达到他。一天清晨，我看到一封来自他直接下属的电子邮件，指出一个技术问题正在影响公司运营。在我向他讲述了我听到的问题后，他问了一个简单的问题：“你从哪里得到这些信息？”我回答说，我是在负责该部门的副总裁那里听到的。他脸上的表情瞬间变得失望。沉默持续了几秒钟。

<span id="page-139-0"></span>Finally, he spoke softly, telling me that his decisions could only be as good as his information.

> 最后，他轻声对我说，他的决定只能和他的信息一样好。

He wasn't getting the raw HiFi information. He knew that people in the organization had an incentive to convey things in a way that covered up mistakes or made themselves look good. And he knew that those filters would obscure rather than clarify the situation.

> 他并没有得到未经修饰的HiFi信息。他清楚地知道，组织中的成员有动机以掩盖错误或让自己看起来更好的方式来传达信息。他也明白，这些过滤机制只会模糊事实，而不会让情况更清晰。

If you want to make better decisions, you need better information. Whenever possible, you need to learn something, see something, or do something for yourself. Sometimes the best information is the least transmissible.

> 如果你想做出更好的决定，就需要更准确的信息。在可能的情况下，你需要自己学习、观察或实践。有时候，最好的信息是最难以传递的。

### 0.3 **HiFi Information Reveals Better Options**

> 29.1.3 高保真信息揭示更优选择

United States general George Marshall was a supremely competent and selfless leader. He never left the welfare of his troops to chance. He valued HiFi information and always went to the source.

> 美国将军乔治·马歇尔是一位极其有能力且无私的领导者。他从不将部队的福祉寄托于偶然。他重视高质量的信息，并总是追本溯源。

At one point during World War II, the War Department ran into a difficult situation with the Air Force in the Pacific: pilots were refusing to fly. The reports Marshall received suggested there was something wrong with the planes. It wasn't a matter of parts. They were getting all the parts they were asking for. Marshall asked if the pilots wanted the planes modified in some way. The American planes were heavier and less maneuverable than the Japanese Zeros, so he had a plane stripped of its armor to reduce its weight. But that wasn't the problem. The pilots didn't want their planes stripped of armor.

> 第二次世界大战期间，美国战争部曾一度在太平洋战场上与空军陷入困境：飞行员们拒绝驾驶飞机。马歇尔收到的报告表明，问题似乎出在飞机上。但这并不是零件短缺的问题，他们已经得到了所有所需零件。马歇尔询问飞行员是否希望对飞机进行某些改装。美国飞机比日本零式战机更重且机动性较差，因此他下令将一架飞机上的装甲拆除以减轻重量。但问题并非如此。飞行员们并不希望自己的飞机被拆除装甲。

Marshall struggled to understand what was going on. Talking to the commander gave him no insight, so he did what he often would: <span id="page-140-2"></span>he sent someone "to look around and see things that weren't being reported—not just what they were yelling about." No one likes the person sent from the head office to check on things—neither the commander nor the line cook. Everyone is suspicious. But Marshall needed eyes and ears on the ground to get to the heart of the matter. He knew he'd only get answers by going directly to the source.

> 马歇尔很难弄清楚到底发生了什么。和指挥官谈话也没有给他带来任何启示，于是他像往常一样采取行动：他派人“去查看一下，看看有没有被隐瞒的情况——不仅仅是那些人们喊叫的事情”。没人喜欢总部派来调查的人，无论是指挥官还是前线厨师，大家都心存疑虑。但马歇尔需要在一线有眼睛和耳朵，才能深入了解事情的真相。他知道，只有直接找到源头，才能得到答案。

What Marshall's direct report uncovered was that the Air Force ground crews didn't have any protection from mosquitoes. They had to work on the planes at night under electric lights, which attracted insects, and those mosquitoes were feasting on them. The mechanics had gotten so full of malaria or antimalarial medication that the pilots didn't trust their work and refused to fly.

> 马歇尔直接下属的调查结果发现，空军地面人员没有任何防蚊措施。他们必须在夜间使用电灯下工作，而这些灯光会吸引昆虫，那些蚊子则在他们身上大量叮咬。维修人员因此感染了疟疾或服用了大量抗疟药物，以至于飞行员对他们失去信任，拒绝让他们驾驶飞机。

The people back at headquarters, in mosquito-protected areas, had no idea what was really going on in the field. They were focused on combat supplies—ammunition, parts, food—but not mosquito netting. With his HiFi information, though, Marshall decided to override a portion of the tonnage they'd allocated for combat supplies and get those nets. Problem solved!

> 总部那些在防蚊区域里的人根本不知道前线到底发生了什么。他们的关注点在于作战物资——弹药、零件、食物，却忽略了蚊帐。不过，凭借他获取的高保真信息，马歇尔决定挪用一部分原本分配给作战物资的运输量，用来获取这些蚊帐。问题迎刃而解！

<span id="page-140-0"></span>Marshall recognized that the only way to understand a problem and solve it was by going to the source. He constantly either went to the front lines himself or sent people he trusted to find out what was really going on.[\[3\]](#page-209-6)

> 马歇尔认识到，要理解并解决问题的唯一途径就是深入问题的源头。他总是亲自前往前线，或者派遣他信任的人去查明真正的情况。[[3]](#page-209-6)

### 0.4 **Making Sure You Get HiFi Information**

> 29.1.4 确保获取HiFi信息

Now that you understand the importance of HiFi information, here are the safeguards for ensuring you always get it.

> 现在你已经了解了HiFi信息的重要性，以下是一些保障措施，确保你始终能够获取到它。

**safeguard:** *Run an experiment.* Try something out to see what kinds of results it yields.

> 保障：运行一个实验。尝试做一些事情，看看会得到什么样的结果。

<span id="page-140-1"></span>An experiment is a low-risk way of gathering important information. For example, if you want to know whether people will pay for something, try to sell it before you even create it. That's what my friends at Tuft & Needle did. They were one of the first companies to ship foam mattresses directly to consumers' homes. They shared an incredible story with me over coffee one day, about their early days. In order to validate their idea, they set up a landing page, bought some Facebook ads, and started taking orders. They didn't even have a product or a company yet; they just wanted to see if people would buy foam mattresses from them. After a few days of receiving orders, they had all the proof they needed that people would buy their product. They refunded all the orders and officially started their company. While this example may be a bit unorthodox, there are many ways in which experimenting can help determine whether there's sufficient demand for a product or service.

> 实验是一种获取重要信息的低风险方式。例如，如果你想了解人们是否愿意为某样东西付费，可以在你甚至还没有创造出这个产品之前，就尝试将其出售。我的朋友在Tuft & Needle公司就是这样做的。他们是最早直接将泡沫床垫运送到消费者家中的一批公司之一。有一天，他们在我喝咖啡时跟我分享了一个令人难以置信的故事，讲述他们创业初期的经历。为了验证他们的想法，他们搭建了一个着陆页面，购买了一些Facebook广告，开始接受订单。他们甚至还没有产品或公司，只是想看看人们是否愿意从他们这里购买泡沫床垫。在几天内收到订单后，他们就获得了足够的证据，证明人们确实愿意购买他们的产品。于是他们退还了所有订单，并正式成立了公司。虽然这个例子可能显得有些非传统，但实验在判断产品或服务是否存在足够市场需求方面，确实有诸多帮助。

**safeguard:** Evaluate the motivations and incentives of your sources. Remember that everyone sees things from a limited perspective.

> 保护：评估你信息来源的动机和利益。请记住，每个人都是从有限的角度来看待事物的。

Evaluating people's motivations and incentives is especially important when you don't have the ability to go and confirm something for yourself. If you absolutely must rely on someone else's information and opinions, you have a responsibility to think about the lens through which they view the situation. Everyone has a limited perspective into the problem. Everyone has a blind spot. It's your job as the decision-maker to weave their perspective together with others to get closer to reality.

> 评估他人的动机和激励因素尤为重要，尤其是在你无法亲自核实情况的时候。如果你必须完全依赖他人提供的信息和观点，就有责任思考他们看待问题的视角。每个人对问题的理解都存在局限，每个人都有自己的盲点。作为决策者，你的任务就是将这些不同的视角综合起来，从而更接近现实。

<span id="page-141-0"></span>A lot of what people consider information or fact is actually just opinion, or a few facts mixed with many opinions. For example, if you're looking to sell your house, everyone who's involved will have a different idea of what you'll make on the sale: the bank, your real estate agent, the buyer's agent, your friends, the home inspector, the internet, and the government. Each of them sees only part of the situation. Each has different motivations and incentives that shape how they see the world. To get a clearer picture of the concrete reality, consider how each person stands to benefit from the information they give you, and weave those perspectives together.

> 人们所认为的信息或事实，实际上很多时候只是观点，或者只是少数事实夹杂着大量意见。例如，如果你想卖掉你的房子，所有参与其中的人都会对这次交易的收益有不同的看法：银行、房地产经纪人、买方代理人、你的朋友、房屋检查员、互联网以及政府。每个人只看到事情的一部分，他们的动机和利益不同，这些因素塑造了他们对世界的看法。要想更清晰地了解现实情况，可以考虑每个人在提供信息时可能获得的利益，将这些观点综合起来。

It helps to think of each person's perspective as a lens onto the

> 将每个人的视角视为一种观察世界的透镜是有帮助的。

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> "lens" 在这里比喻为“透镜”，意指每个人的观点就像一种过滤器，影响着他们对世界的看法。

world. When you put their glasses on, you see what they see and have better insight into what they might be feeling. But those glasses have blind spots, often missing important information or confusing fact with opinion. By trying on all the glasses, you see what others miss.

> 世界。当你戴上他们的“眼镜”时，你就能看到他们所看到的，并更好地理解他们可能感受到的情绪。但这些“眼镜”也有盲点，常常遗漏重要信息，或将事实与观点混淆。通过尝试所有这些“眼镜”，你就能看到别人所忽略的内容。

When you're getting information from other people, you need to keep an open mind. That means withholding your own judgment as long as possible. People often undermine the information-gathering process by subjecting others to their judgments, beliefs, and perspective. The point isn't to argue or disagree, however. Judging people and telling them they're wrong only shuts them down and prevents a free flow of information. When you're gathering information, your job is to see the world through other people's eyes. You're trying to understand their experience and how they processed it. You can learn valuable information even when you don't agree with their view of the world. Just ask questions, keep your thoughts to yourself, and remain curious about other perspectives.

> 当你从他人那里获取信息时，你需要保持开放的心态。这意味着尽可能地克制自己的判断。人们常常通过将自己的判断、信念和视角强加于他人，从而削弱信息收集的过程。然而，重点并不是去争论或不同意。评判他人、告诉他们错了只会让他们关闭自己，阻碍信息的自由流动。当你在收集信息时，你的任务是设身处地地从他人的角度去看世界。你试图理解他们的经历以及他们是如何处理这些经历的。即使你不同意他们对世界的看法，你也能从中获得有价值的信息。只需多提问，保持沉默，对不同的观点保持好奇。

**safeguard:** When you get information from other people, ask questions that yield detailed answers. Don't ask people what they think; instead, ask them *how* they think.

> 保障：当你从他人那里获取信息时，提出能够获得详细回答的问题。不要问人们他们认为如何，而是问他们是如何思考的。

If you ask people what to do in a given situation, you might get the correct answer, but you haven't learned anything. Say a local government task force needs to hire a software developer for a project, but they have no experience doing so and don't know what to look for. Person A on the task force goes to a developer friend and asks, "Whom should I hire for this project?" Person B does the same but instead says, "I'm hiring a software developer, and I'd like to learn from your experiences. What skills matter and which ones can be learned on the job? Why? Where do I find the best people? How do I test these skills?" And so on.

> 如果你向人们询问在特定情况下应该怎么做，你可能会得到正确的答案，但你并没有真正学到什么。例如，假设一个地方政府工作组需要为一个项目招聘一名软件开发人员，但他们没有任何相关经验，也不清楚该找什么样的人。工作组中的A成员去找一个开发朋友，问：“我应该招聘谁来负责这个项目？”而B成员则做了不同的事情，他不仅问：“我要招聘一名软件开发人员，我想从你的经验中学习。哪些技能是关键的？哪些技能可以在工作中学习？为什么？我应该在哪里找到最优秀的人？我该如何测试这些技能？”等等。

Person B might not have a recommendation within the first conversation, but I'd give it ten-to-one odds that they find a better candidate in the end. The reason: Person B is asking about the principles that guide decision-making in this domain, not details about the specific case. They're asking others about their earned knowledge and making it their own.

> 人B可能在第一次交谈中并没有推荐人选，但我打赌十比一，他们最终会找到更合适的人选。原因在于：人B询问的是指导该领域决策的原则，而不是具体案例的细节。他们是在向他人请教已获得的知识，并将其转化为自己的见解。

Our goal in decision-making is not just to gather information, but to gather information relevant to our decision. That requires more than building an inventory of data points; it requires understanding the why and how behind those data points—the principles that good decision-makers use in this area.

> 我们做决策的目标不仅仅是收集信息，而是收集与决策相关的信息。这需要的不仅仅是积累数据点的清单，还需要理解这些数据点背后的缘由和机制——这是优秀决策者在这一领域所使用的原理。

Getting at those principles requires asking the right kinds of questions. There are three I'd recommend:

> 要把握这些原则，需要提出正确类型的问题。我推荐以下三个问题：

- **Question 1:** What are the variables you'd use to make this decision if you were in my shoes? How do those variables relate to one another?
- <span id="page-143-0"></span>**Question 2:** What do you know about this problem that I (or other people) don't? What can you see based on your experience that someone without your experience can't? What do you know that most people miss?
- **Question 3:** What would be your process for deciding if you were in my shoes? How would you go about doing it? (Or: How would you tell your mother/friend to go about doing it?)

> 问题一：如果你站在我的位置上，你会使用哪些变量来做出这个决定？这些变量之间是如何相互关联的？

问题二：你了解这个问題的哪些方面是我（或其他人）所不了解的？基于你的经验，你能看到别人没有经验的人看不到的东西是什么？你了解的哪些内容是大多数人忽略的？

问题三：如果你站在我的位置上，你的决策过程会是怎样的？你会如何进行？（或者：你会如何告诉你的母亲或朋友去进行？）

Notice how different these questions are from the typical, "Here's my problem. What should I do?" Remember: the questions you ask help to determine the quality of the information you get.

> 注意这些提问方式与通常的“我遇到了一个问题，我该怎么办？”有多么不同。请记住：你提出的问题将决定你所获得信息的质量。

#### 0.4.1 **Getting Accurate Information from Experts**

> 29.1.4.1 从专家处获取准确信息

We've talked about the importance of getting high-fidelity information. The second principle for getting accurate information is getting high-expertise information:

> 我们已经讨论了获取高保真信息的重要性。获取准确信息的第二个原则是获取高专业度的信息：

**the hiex principle:** Get high-expertise (HiEx) information, which comes both from people with a lot of knowledge and/or experience in a specific area, and from people with knowledge and experience in many areas.

> HiEx原则：获取高专业性（HiEx）信息，这类信息既来自在特定领域拥有大量知识和/或经验的人，也来自在多个领域都具备知识和经验的人。

When someone close to the problem isn't available to you, look for people who recently solved a similar problem. The word "recent" is an important nuance here. When you want specific advice from an expert, look for someone who recently solved the problem you're trying to solve. Asking someone who solved your problem twenty years ago how they did it is not likely to offer specific and effective insights. You want a current expert—and no, I don't mean the talking heads on TV. They're rarely actual experts.

> 当遇到问题时，若无法直接向相关人士寻求帮助，应寻找那些最近解决过类似问题的人。这里的“最近”是一个非常关键的细微差别。当你需要专家的具体建议时，应该寻找那些最近解决过你所面临问题的人。向二十年前解决过你这个问题的人请教，不太可能获得具体且有效的见解。你真正需要的是一个当前的专家——不，我并不是指电视上的那些评论员。他们很少真正是专家。

Experts can increase the accuracy of your information and decrease the time it takes to get it. Getting even one expert's advice can cut through a lot of confusion and help you quickly formulate and/or eliminate options.

> 专家可以提高您信息的准确性，并减少获取信息所需的时间。即使获得一位专家的建议，也能帮助您迅速厘清困惑，快速制定或排除选项。

I learned the value of expert advice firsthand when I began coding at an intelligence agency. It was a very different experience from coding as I'd learned it. In school, it was possible to basically just Google things and piece them together. People had solved these problems long ago and the solutions hadn't changed much. My job at the intelligence agency was much harder. Not only were we prohibited from Googling anything we were coding for security reasons, but even if we'd been allowed to, it wouldn't have helped: we were trying to do things no one had ever done before.

> 当我开始在情报机构从事编程工作时，我亲身体会到了专家建议的价值。这与我以前在学校里学到的编程体验完全不同。在学校里，基本上可以通过谷歌查找资料，然后把它们拼凑起来解决问题。人们早就解决了这些问题，而且解决方案几乎没有变化。而在情报机构，我的工作要困难得多。我们不仅因为安全原因被禁止搜索任何与我们编程相关的内容，即使允许搜索，也不会有任何帮助：我们尝试做的是以前从未有人做过的事情。

A few months in, I got stuck on a problem. Really stuck! As a kid I'd always taken in a lot of different perspectives on a problem, but in the end, I always thought if I just put my head down and worked harder, I'd figure it out eventually. Days went by. Then weeks. I couldn't understand what was happening. Finally, with my head down, I approached someone who had worked on a similar problem before and explained what I was stuck on.

> 几个月后，我遇到了一个难题，真的卡住了！小时候，我总是从不同的角度看待问题，但最终我总是认为，只要埋头苦干，努力工作，最终总会弄明白的。日子一天天过去，接着是星期又星期，我始终无法理解到底发生了什么。最后，我仍然埋头苦干，却去找了一位曾经解决过类似问题的人，向他解释我到底卡在了哪里。

<span id="page-144-0"></span>"Let me look at your code," he said. In less than twenty minutes he diagnosed what was wrong: there was a subtle difference between what the documentation said would happen and what would actually happen in certain edge cases. Since most people wouldn't run into those edge cases, the problem wasn't documented anywhere. This person had faced and overcome the same problem, though, and it had taken him a long time to solve. He was happy to share his hard-earned knowledge. While I was a bit frustrated that I'd wasted weeks out of stubbornness, this exchange kick-started our relationship, and I learned a lot from him over the years.

> “让我看看你的代码，”他说。不到二十分钟，他就诊断出了问题所在：文档中说明的行为与某些极端情况下的实际行为之间存在微妙的差异。由于大多数人不会遇到这些极端情况，因此这个问题并未被记录在任何文档中。然而，这个人曾经遇到并克服了同样的问题，而解决它花费了他很长时间。他很乐意分享自己苦心获得的经验。虽然我因为固执而浪费了几周时间感到有些沮丧，但这次交流却重新点燃了我们之间的关系，多年来我从他那里学到了很多东西。

Even one expert's opinion can be more helpful than the thoughts and guesses of dozens or hundreds of amateurs. But how do you recruit one to work with you?

> 即使一位专家的意见，也可能比几十甚至几百名业余爱好者的想法和猜测更有帮助。但你如何才能招募到这样一位专家为你工作呢？

I've experienced expert advice from both sides: getting and giving. I reach out to experts all the time for insight, and I have thousands of people who reach out to me for advice. Let me share what I've learned on both recruiting experts and working with them.

> 我曾从两个角度体验过专家建议：获取和提供。我经常向专家寻求见解，同时也有成千上的人向我寻求建议。让我分享一下我在招募专家以及与他们合作方面学到的经验。

#### 0.4.2 **Getting Experts on Your Side**

> 29.1.4.2 争取专家支持

Many people don't want to reach out to experts for help, either because they don't think it's an option, or because they're afraid of being a nuisance. Sometimes, if we know the expert, we're embarrassed. Maybe they'll discover we know less than we actually do!

> 很多人不愿意向专家求助，不是因为他们觉得这不是一种选择，就是因为他们担心会打扰到专家。有时候，如果我们认识这位专家，就会感到不好意思。也许他们会发现我们其实懂得的并不多！

If you have any anxieties of this sort, the first thing to understand is that experts *love* sharing what they've learned when they know it'll make a difference. Helping others achieve their goals is one of the things that make life and work meaningful. To put it in perspective, think of a time in your life when someone asked you for help on something you excel at, and you came through for them. How did it feel? For most of us, sharing expertise feels pretty good. We enjoy exercising an ability we have, and we also enjoy gaining recognition for having it.

> 如果你有这种焦虑，首先要明白的是，专家们非常乐意分享他们所学到的知识，尤其是当他们知道这些知识会对他人产生积极影响时。帮助他人实现他们的目标，是让生活和工作变得有意义的重要部分。为了更清楚地理解这一点，回想一下你生命中有没有某个时刻，有人向你求助，而你恰好擅长那方面，你最终帮助了他们。当时你感觉如何？对大多数人来说，分享自己的专长是一件非常令人愉快的事。我们享受运用自己的能力，也享受因拥有这些能力而得到的认可。

<span id="page-145-0"></span>Experts don't treat all requests for help equally, though. Some requests really don't feel good to receive. Usually these are requests of the tell-me-what-I-should-do type. Often these people haven't done the work ahead of time, they just want you to decide for them. I get hundreds—if not thousands—of these requests a year. People want me to solve their problems for them. They send twenty pages of thoughts and say, "What should I do?"[\[\\*\]](#page-282-0)

> 尽管专家们不会平等地对待所有求助请求，但有些请求确实让人感到不舒服。通常这些请求属于“告诉我该怎么做”的类型。这些人往往没有提前做功课，只是希望你替他们做决定。我每年会收到数百——甚至上千——这样的请求。人们希望我替他们解决问题。他们寄来二十页的思绪，然后问：“我该怎么办？”

<span id="page-145-1"></span>Remember: the goal isn't to have someone tell you what to do; rather, it's to learn how an expert thinks about the problem, which variables they consider relevant, and how those variables interact over time. If you present a problem, and an expert simply tells you what to do, they're just giving you an abstraction. You might get the answer right, but you haven't learned anything. And if things go wrong, which they inevitably will, you won't have a clue as to why. You're the line cook masquerading as the chef. If you ask them *how* they think about the problem, that's when you start deepening your understanding.

> 记住：我们的目标不是让某人告诉你该怎么做；而是学习专家是如何思考这个问题的，他们考虑哪些变量是相关的，以及这些变量是如何随时间相互作用的。如果你提出一个问题，而专家只是直接告诉你该怎么做，那他们只是在给你一个抽象的答案。你可能会得到正确的答案，但你实际上并没有学到任何东西。而且，如果事情出了问题（而这种情况终究会发生），你将完全不知道原因。你不过是一个假装成主厨的切菜工。只有当你向他们请教他们是如何思考这个问题的，你才能真正加深对问题的理解。

So let's talk about how to approach an expert in a way that will set your request apart and get people excited to help you. Here are five tips:

> 那么，让我们谈谈如何以一种能让你的请求脱颖而出，并激发人们乐于助你的方式去接触专家。以下是五个建议：

- **Show that you have skin in the game:** When you reach out to an expert, make them aware of the time, energy, and money you've already invested in the problem. Let them know you've done the work and that you're stuck. When I see requests from someone who shows they're invested in solving a problem, and who demonstrates they've done their research to craft a pitch around a very specific issue I can help with, I'm happy and eager to respond. Contrast that with emails that say, "Hey Shane, what do you think of this investment opportunity?" Which would you be more excited to answer?
- **Get precise on your ask:** Be very clear what you're looking for. Are you looking for them to review your plan and provide feedback? Are you looking for them to introduce you to people who can solve the problem? Whatever it is that you want, just be clear.
- **Show respect for their time and energy:** Explicitly stating that the person you're reaching out to is an expert whose time and energy you respect goes a long way to secure their goodwill. You should also demonstrate your respect for them, though. For instance, do not ask for fifteen minutes to pick their brain; instead, ask if they offer one-off consulting sessions and how much they charge for them. Experts are expensive and most of the time for good reason. If you're paying \$1,000 to \$2,000 per hour for something, it forces you to get clear on what you want before you hop on the call. Paying for someone's time not only compensates them for the value they bring to the table but forces you to make sure you're not mumbling through

> 展示你真正投入其中：当你向专家求助时，要让他们知道你已经为解决问题投入了时间、精力和金钱。让他们知道你已经做了工作，但遇到了困难。当我看到有人展现出他们对解决问题的投入，并且展示了他们在特定问题上的深入研究，从而提出一个具体请求时，我会感到高兴并迫不及待地回应。这与那些邮件中说“嘿，Shane，你觉得这个投资机会怎么样？”的请求形成鲜明对比。你更愿意回应哪一种呢？

明确你的请求：清楚地表达你想要什么。你是希望他们审阅你的计划并提供反馈？还是希望他们介绍能解决问题的人？无论你想要什么，都一定要清晰表达。

尊重他们的时间和精力：明确表达你联系的人是一位专家，并且你尊重他们的时间和精力，这在赢得他们的善意方面非常有效。你也要表现出对他们的尊重。例如，不要要求花十五分钟“跟他们脑力激荡”，而是询问他们是否提供单次咨询，以及收费多少。专家的时间很宝贵，大多数时候这也是合理的。如果你每小时要支付2000元，这会迫使你在通话前就明确自己想要什么。为别人的时间付费不仅补偿了他们带来的价值，也迫使你确保自己不是在胡言乱语。

the call and wasting their time and yours.

> 打电话并浪费你和他们的时间。

- **Ask for their reasons and listen:** As mentioned previously, don't just ask experts what they think, ask them how they think. Use them as a resource to train yourself how to evaluate things so that you can start embodying an expert way of operating. You don't have to agree with what they're saying, but remember: your goal is to learn from them how to think better, not to have them solve your problem for you.
- <span id="page-147-0"></span>• **Follow up:** If you want to build a network and make this more than a transactional request, follow up to report on your progress no matter what the outcome is. Whether their advice helped you in this case or not, following up and keeping them updated on your progress primes them to help you in the future. When they see that you took their advice seriously, they're going to want to help you again.

> 询问他们的理由并倾听：如前所述，不要仅仅询问专家们的想法，而要问他们是如何思考的。将他们作为资源，用来训练自己如何评估事物，从而开始具备专家的工作方式。你不必完全同意他们所说的话，但请记住：你的目标是向他们学习如何更有效地思考，而不是让他们来替你解决问题。
• 跟进反馈：如果你想建立人脉关系，并让这次请求超越单纯的交易行为，无论结果如何，都要跟进并汇报你的进展。无论他们的建议是否在这个案例中对你有所帮助，跟进并及时更新你的进展，会让他们更愿意在未来帮助你。当他们看到你认真对待他们的建议时，他们就会更愿意再次帮助你。

Of course, most experts aren't able to respond to everyone who needs their help. It's much easier if you develop a personal relationship with people before you need their help. That way the request isn't purely transactional. It's impossible to predict which fields you might someday need an expert in, but that's one reason to cast a wide net socially and professionally. I just looked at my inbox last week and had fifty-three requests for "help" in one form or another. Two were from friends. I can't reply to them all, so where do you think my time will go?

> 当然，大多数专家都无法满足每一个需要他们帮助的人。如果你在需要帮助之前就与这些人建立起个人关系，那么事情会容易得多。这样，你的请求就不再仅仅是一次性的交易行为。你无法预知将来可能会需要哪方面的专家，但这也是一个理由，让你在社交和职业领域广泛拓展人脉。我上周刚刚查看了一下邮箱，发现有一百零三个请求以各种形式寻求“帮助”，其中有两个来自朋友。我无法一一回复，那么你认为我的时间会花在哪儿呢？

### 0.5 **Experts vs. Imitators**

> 29.1.5 专家与模仿者

Getting HiEx information requires that you get help from real experts. But there are many people who claim to be experts (or whom other people claim to be experts) who really aren't.

> 获取HiEx信息需要借助真正专家的帮助。但有很多人声称自己是专家（或被他人声称是专家），实际上却并非如此。

**safeguard:** *Take time to distinguish real experts from imitators.* Not everyone who claims to be an expert is. Take the time to know the difference.

> 保障：花时间区分真正的专家和模仿者。并不是每个声称自己是专家的人都真正具备这种能力。花时间了解其中的区别。

Think of all the money managers who borrow their talking points

> 想想那些借来谈论点子的钱经理们。

from Warren Buffett. They might sound like Buffett, but they don't know how to invest the way Buffett does. They're imitators. Charlie Munger once commented on this: "It's very hard to tell the difference between a good money manager and someone who just has the patter down."

> 来自沃伦·巴菲特。他们听起来可能像巴菲特，但并不真正了解巴菲特的投资方式。他们只是模仿者。查理·芒格曾对此做过评论：“很难区分一位优秀的资金管理者和一个仅仅掌握了套话的人。”

<span id="page-148-0"></span>But what if you're not an expert yourself? How do you tell the difference between an expert and an imitator?

> 但如果你自己也不是专家呢？你如何区分真正的专家和模仿者？

Experts are usually enthusiastic about their area of expertise. That's why they're good at it: they spend even their spare time mastering and refining their knowledge and skills, and it shows. Imitators are less concerned with being great and more concerned with looking great. That concern makes it easy for the ego to take over.

> 专家通常对自己的专业领域充满热情。正因如此，他们才擅长这一领域：他们甚至在闲暇时间也会不断钻研和精进自己的知识与技能，这种努力也自然体现在他们的表现中。而模仿者则更关注如何看起来出色，而非真正变得卓越。这种关注很容易让自大情绪占据上风。

Here are some things to look for:

> 这里有一些需要注意的事项：

- <span id="page-148-1"></span>• **Imitators can't answer questions at a deeper level.** Specific knowledge is earned, not learned, so imitators don't fully understand the ideas they're talking about.[\[\\*\]](#page-283-0) Their knowledge is shallow. As a result, when you ask about details, or first principles or nonstandard cases, they don't have good answers.
- **Imitators can't adapt their vocabulary.** They can explain things using only the vocabulary they were taught, which is often full of jargon. Because they don't fully understand the ideas behind the vocabulary, they can't adapt the way they talk about those ideas to express them more clearly to their audience.
- **Imitators get frustrated when you say you don't understand.** That frustration is a result of being overly concerned with the appearance of expertise—which they might not be able to maintain if they have to really get into the weeds with an explanation. Real experts have earned their expertise and are excited about trying to share what they know. They aren't frustrated by your lack of understanding; they instead love your genuine curiosity about something they care about.
- **Experts can tell you all the ways they've failed.** They know and accept that some form of failure is often part of the learning process. Imitators, however, are less likely to own up to

> • 模仿者无法在更深层次回答问题。具体知识是通过实践获得的，而不是单纯通过学习获得的，因此模仿者并不真正理解他们所谈论的概念。他们的知识较为肤浅。因此，当你问及细节、第一性原理或非标准情况时，他们往往没有好的答案。

模仿者无法灵活运用自己的词汇。他们只能用被教导过的词汇来解释事物，而这些词汇往往充满术语。由于他们并未真正理解这些词汇背后的概念，因此无法调整自己的表达方式，以更清晰地向受众传达这些概念。

当你表示自己不理解时，模仿者会感到沮丧。这种沮丧源于他们过于在意表面上的专业形象——如果他们必须深入解释，他们可能无法维持这种形象。真正专家所拥有的专业能力是通过实践获得的，他们乐于分享自己的知识。他们不会因为你的不理解而感到沮丧，反而会欣赏你对某些他们关心的事物所表现出的真诚好奇心。

专家可以告诉你他们失败的各种方式。他们知道并接受失败通常是学习过程的一部分。然而，模仿者却不太可能承认这一点。

- mistakes because they're afraid it will tarnish the image they're trying to project.
- **Imitators don't know the limits of their expertise.** Experts know what they know, and also know what they don't know. They understand that their understanding has boundaries, and they're able to tell you when they're approaching the limits of their circle of competence. Imitators can't. They can't tell when they're crossing the boundary into things they don't understand.

> 他们之所以犯错，是因为他们害怕这会损害他们试图塑造的形象。模仿者并不了解自身专业能力的边界。专家清楚自己知道什么，也清楚自己不知道什么。他们明白自己的理解是有局限的，并且能够告诉你何时接近了自己专业能力的边界。模仿者则做不到这一点。他们无法判断自己是否已经越过了自己不了解的领域。  

>[!note]- 翻译注释  
> "circle of competence" 翻译为“专业能力的边界”或“能力范围”，意指个人或组织在特定领域内具备的知识和技能范围。

A final note on distinguishing experts from imitators: Many of us learn about a subject not by reading original research or listening to the expert for hours, but by reading something intended to be highly transmissible. Think again of the difference between reading an academic article and reading a newspaper article about it. While they know more than the layman, popularizers are not experts themselves. Instead, they are good at clearly and memorably communicating ideas. As a result, popularizers often get mistaken for experts. Keep that in mind when you're in the market for an expert: the person with real expertise is often not the person who made the subject popular.

> 关于区分专家与模仿者，最后再提一点：我们中许多人并不是通过阅读原始研究或长时间聆听专家来学习某个学科的，而是通过阅读一些旨在易于传播的内容。再想想学术文章和关于该主题的新闻报道之间的区别。虽然他们比普通大众知道得更多，但普及者本身并不是专家。相反，他们擅长清晰而令人难忘地传达思想。因此，普及者常常被误认为是专家。当你在寻找专家时，请记住这一点：真正具备专业知识的人，往往并不是让这个学科变得流行的人。

>[!note]- 翻译注释  
> "popularizers" 翻译为“普及者”或“通俗化者”，指那些将专业知识以通俗易懂的方式传播给大众的人。

### 0.6 **CHAPTER 4.4**

> 4.4 章节

# 31 **Do it!**

> 做吧！

<span id="page-150-0"></span>You've considered the options. You've evaluated them. You've found the best. It's time to act!

> 你已经考虑过各种选择，评估过它们，找到了最优解。现在，是采取行动的时候了！

There is no purpose to knowing what you should do and not doing it. If you want results, you need action.

> 知道该做什么却不去做，这样做毫无意义。如果你想获得结果，就必须采取行动。

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> “knowing what you should do and not doing it” 译为“知道该做什么却不去做”，保留了原句的对比和强调效果。

Making a judgment and executing it is easier than it seems and harder than others imagine. One reason we fail to take action is that we're scared to deal with the consequences. It's not so much that we don't know what to do as much as we don't want to deal with the reality of doing it. We don't want to have conversations, because they might hurt people's feelings. We don't want to fire the person that we like, even though we know they're wrong for the job.

> 做出判断并执行它比看起来容易，却比他人想象的更难。我们之所以迟迟不采取行动，其中一个原因是害怕面对后果。并非我们不知道该怎么做，而是我们不愿面对去做的现实。我们不愿进行对话，因为这可能会伤害别人的感情。我们也不愿解雇那个我们喜欢的人，尽管我们知道他并不适合这份工作。

Our ego conspires with the social default and the inertia default to weaken our resolve and keep us from doing what we need to do. But that's not the only reason we fail to act.

> 我们的自尊心与社会默认的惯性联手，削弱我们的决心，使我们无法去做本该做的事情。但那并不是我们迟迟不采取行动的唯一原因。

Another big reason we find action hard is that we're afraid of being wrong. In this case inertia holds us in place as we gather more and more information in the false hope that we can ultimately eliminate uncertainty.

> 我们难以采取行动的另一个重要原因是我们害怕犯错。在这种情况下，惯性让我们原地踏步，我们不断收集更多信息，却在虚假的希望中试图最终消除不确定性。

<span id="page-150-1"></span>There are three principles that can help you know when to stop deliberating and start acting. But first, let's discuss a helpful way of categorizing decisions by considering how *consequential* they are, and how *reversible* they are.

> 有三个原则可以帮助你判断何时该停止反复思虑，开始行动。但在那之前，让我们先讨论一种有助于分类决策的方法，即考虑这些决策的影响程度以及可逆性。

### 0.1 **Consequentiality and Reversibility**

> 30.1.1 后果性与可逆性

Consequential decisions affect the things that matter most: whom you marry, where you live, which business you launch. The more a decision affects what matters to you—either in the short term or the long term—the more consequential it is.

> 决定性的选择会影响那些最重要的事情：你娶谁、住在哪里、创办哪家企业。一个决定影响你所看重的事物越多——无论是短期还是长期——它就越具决定性。

Reversible decisions can be undone by a later course of action. The harder or more costly it is to undo a decision's effects, the less reversible it is. It's easy to eat a chocolate bar, but once you've eaten it, it's done. You can't undo it. Having a baby is the same. Once you have one, you can't undo it (nor would you want to!). At the other extreme would be a decision whose effects cost nothing to undo. I can decide to sign up for a free fourteen-day trial of something, knowing full well it's easy to undo.

> 可逆的决定可以通过后续的行为来撤销。撤销决定所产生的影响越困难或成本越高，该决定就越不可逆。吃巧克力条很容易，但一旦你吃了，就无法挽回。生孩子也是如此。一旦有了孩子，你无法撤销这个决定（而且你也不会想这么做！）。相反，有些决定的影响撤销起来几乎不花任何成本。我可以决定报名参加某个东西的免费14天试用，我清楚地知道这很容易撤销。

We can represent different kinds of decisions in terms of their degrees of consequence and reversibility on a graph (see the following figure). Among these decisions, two types deserve special attention: decisions that are highly consequential and irreversible, and decisions that are inconsequential and highly reversible.

> 我们可以用一个图表来表示不同类型的决策，根据其后果的程度以及可逆性（见下图）。在这些决策中，有两类特别值得关注：一是后果严重且不可逆的决策，二是后果轻微且高度可逆的决策。

When a decision is highly consequential and irreversible, its effects ripple throughout your life, and there's no way to stop them. Some people call these "lead dominoes."

> 当一个决定具有重大且不可逆转的影响时，其后果将贯穿你的一生，且无法阻止。一些人称之为“关键多米诺骨牌”。

When decisions are like lead dominoes, the cost of a mistake is high. Exactly the opposite is true of a decision that's inconsequential and easily reversed. The cost of a mistake is low: if you don't like the outcome, you can just reverse it. The biggest mistake in cases like these is wasting time and mental energy. If you can take something back, or it doesn't really matter, continuing to gather information becomes a drain on resources.

> 如果一个决定像铅制多米诺骨牌一样，犯错的成本就很高。而如果一个决定无关紧要且很容易撤销，情况则完全相反。犯错的成本很低：如果你不喜欢结果，只需撤销即可。在这些情况下，最大的错误就是浪费时间和精力。如果你能够撤销决定，或者结果并不重要，继续收集信息就会消耗资源。

![](IMG-20260408090242614.jpeg)

If you've ever bought a mattress, you'll know exactly what I mean. You spend hours—if not days—looking at mattresses, reading reviews, comparing prices, and considering whether you're a hot or cold sleeper. You finally decide on a mattress, and have it delivered, only to find that it isn't what you dreamed of. So you exchange it for your fallback option anyway. You could have saved hours or days simply ensuring the store had a flexible return policy, deciding on a mattress within an hour, and moving on. When the cost of a mistake is low, move fast.

> 如果你曾经买过床垫，你就会明白我的意思。你会花好几个小时，甚至几天的时间去挑选床垫，阅读评论，比较价格，并思考自己是热睡眠者还是冷睡眠者。最终你选定一款床垫，把它送到家中，却发现它远不如你想象中理想。于是你只能无奈地换回原本备选的床垫。其实，如果你确保店铺有灵活的退货政策，只需花一个小时就做出决定，然后继续前进，就能节省大量时间。当错误的成本很低时，就该迅速行动。

### 0.2 <span id="page-152-0"></span>**Three Principles for Action**

> 30.1.2 行动的三大原则

Now that we have a way of categorizing decisions by their degrees of consequence and revisability, let's talk about some principles. The first is this:

> 现在我们有了一个方法，可以按照决策的后果程度和可修正性对决策进行分类，接下来让我们谈谈一些原则。第一个原则是：

**the asap principle:** If the cost to undo the decision is low, make it as soon as possible.

> 尽快原则：如果撤销决定的成本很低，就尽快做出决定。

In fact, if something is too inconsequential, then engaging in *any*

> 事实上，如果某件事太过微不足道，那么参与其中就毫无意义。

decision-making might be a waste. Just choose. Decide quickly, and learn by doing. You'll save time, energy, and resources that you can use for decisions that really matter.

> 决策可能会浪费时间。直接做出选择吧。迅速决定，通过实践来学习。这样你可以节省时间、精力和资源，用在真正重要的决定上。

If, on the other hand, the decision is highly consequential and irreversible, then the stakes are high. The biggest risk here is moving too fast and missing something important. You want to gather as much information as you can before deciding. Therefore, the second principle is:

> 如果决策具有重大影响且不可逆转，那么风险就非常高。此时最大的风险是行动过快而忽略了重要事项。因此，在做出决定之前，你应尽可能多地收集信息。因此，第二个原则是：

**the alap principle:** If the cost to undo a decision is high, make it as late as possible.

> 阿尔帕原则：如果撤销一个决定的成本很高，就尽量将这个决定推迟到最晚的时候做出。

Remember to factor the cost of analysis into your decisions. This is something many people fail to do. Most decisions require an art that balances speed and accuracy. When you move too slowly on small decisions, you waste time and energy, no matter how accurate you may be. When you go too fast, you miss crucial information, make assumptions, overlook the basics, rush to judgment, and often solve the wrong problem. When things are hectic, however—even when speed matters—you need to slow down, just a little.

> 记住在做决定时要考虑到分析的成本。这是许多人常常忽略的一点。大多数决定都需要一种艺术，来平衡速度与准确性。当你在处理小决定时行动过于缓慢，无论你多么准确，都会浪费时间和精力。而当你行动太快时，就会错过关键信息，做出假设，忽视基本问题，仓促下结论，常常解决的是错误的问题。然而，当事情变得紧张——即使速度很重要——你也需要稍微放慢一点节奏。

<span id="page-153-1"></span><span id="page-153-0"></span>Michael Lewis gives an example of this in *The Undoing Project* about a woman who crashed head on into another car.[\[1\]](#page-209-7) Medics rushed her to Sunnybrook Hospital, which is located next to Canada's busiest stretch of highway. Sunnybrook had a reputation for treating the emergencies and traumas that come from car crashes, yet the woman had so many broken bones, the physicians missed some. Don Redelmeier was the Sunnybrook epidemiologist. His job was to "check the understanding of the specialists for mental errors." He was there, in other words, to check other people's thinking. "Wherever there is uncertainty there has got to be judgment," said Redelmeier, "and wherever there is judgment there is an opportunity for human fallibility." Doctors may be experts, but they're still human, still fallible, and to complicate things further, their patients often give them unreliable information.

> 迈克尔·刘易斯在他的著作《逆转的项目》中举了一个例子，讲述了一位女性正面撞上另一辆车的情形。急救人员迅速将她送往位于加拿大最繁忙高速公路旁边的辛尼布罗克医院。辛尼布罗克医院以处理车祸带来的紧急情况和创伤而闻名，但这位女士伤势严重，多处骨折，医生们却遗漏了一些伤情。唐·雷德尔迈尔是辛尼布罗克医院的流行病学家。他的职责是“检查专家们对精神错误的理解”。换句话说，他就是来检查他人思维方式的。雷德尔迈尔曾说：“在存在不确定性的任何地方，都必须有判断；而在存在判断的地方，就存在人类犯错的机会。”医生们可能都是专家，但他们仍然是人类，仍然会犯错。更复杂的是，他们的病人常常提供不可靠的信息。

When things move fast, and life-and-death decisions need to be

> 当事情发展迅速，生死攸关的决定需要做出时，

made, we often see only the things we're specifically trained to see, and miss others that are nevertheless relevant. In this case, the woman presented another problem beyond all the broken bones: her heartbeat was highly irregular. Before she lost consciousness, she mentioned a history of an overactive thyroid, which is a classic cause of an irregular heartbeat.

> 我们往往只能看到那些我们被特别训练去注意的事物，而忽略了其他同样相关的内容。在这个案例中，这位妇女除了所有骨折之外，还出现了另一个问题：她的心跳极其不规则。在她失去意识之前，她提到自己有甲状腺功能亢进的病史，而这也是心跳不规则的典型原因之一。

Redelmeier entered as the team looking after her was preparing to administer the drugs for hyperthyroidism: "[He] asked everyone to slow down. To wait. Just a moment. Just to check their thinking —and to make sure they were not trying to force the facts into an easy, coherent, but ultimately false story."

> 红德尔迈尔进入时，负责她的医疗团队正准备给患者注射治疗甲状腺功能亢进的药物。她说：“他让所有人都放慢速度，等待一下。只是一会儿，只是为了检查他们的思考——并确保他们没有试图将事实强行套入一个简单、连贯但却最终是错误的故事中。”

He wanted to slow things down because they had leaped to a conclusion that seemed to fit without considering other reasons: "Hyperthyroidism is a classic cause of an irregular heart rhythm, but hyperthyroidism is an infrequent cause of an irregular heart rhythm," he would say later. While it fit, it was unlikely—possible but not probable.

> 他想放慢节奏，因为他们已经跳到了一个看似合理却未考虑其他原因的结论上：“甲状腺功能亢进症是心律不齐的一个经典病因，但甲状腺功能亢进症却是心律不齐的一个罕见原因。”他后来会这么说。虽然这个结论在逻辑上成立，但可能性很低——虽然有可能，但不太可能。

The staff began searching for other causes, and quickly determined she had a collapsed lung. "Like her fractured ribs, her collapsed lung had failed to turn up on the X-ray. Unlike the fractured ribs, it could kill her." They ignored the thyroid and treated the collapsed lung, and her heartbeat returned to normal. When her official thyroid tests came back the next day, they were normal. As Redelmeier said, "You need to be so careful when there is one simple diagnosis that instantly pops into your mind that beautifully explains everything all at once. That's when you need to stop and check your thinking."

> 工作人员开始寻找其他原因，很快确定她患有肺部塌陷。“和她骨折的肋骨一样，她的肺部塌陷在X光检查中也没有显现出来。但与骨折的肋骨不同，它可能会要了她的命。”他们忽略了甲状腺问题，转而治疗肺部塌陷，她的脉搏逐渐恢复正常。第二天，她的甲状腺检查结果也显示正常。正如雷德尔迈尔所说：“当你脑海中突然浮现出一个简单而完美的诊断，能够瞬间解释一切时，你必须格外小心。这时，你就要停下来，仔细检查自己的思维。”

When the stakes are high, and there are no take-backs, you want to decide at the last moment possible, and keep as many options on the table as you can while continuing to gather information.

> 当局势紧张且无法回头时，你希望在最后一刻做出决定，并尽可能保留更多选择，同时继续收集信息。

In driver's education, we learn that when you're on the freeway driving at high speeds, you need to keep a pocket open in front of you in case someone unexpectedly veers into your lane or stops abruptly. Keeping extra distance between cars allows you to keep options open for whatever might happen. This is the same reason you should wait as long as possible when making an important decision. You want to give yourself as many options in the future so that if something changes, you have the space to maneuver and reposition yourself along the path of greatest opportunity.

> 在驾驶课程中，我们学到，当你在高速公路上以高速行驶时，需要在前方保持一个“安全距离”，以防有人突然变道进入你的车道或紧急刹车。保持与前车的距离，可以为你留出更多的应对空间，以应对可能发生的情况。同样的道理也适用于你在做重要决定时，应尽可能等待更长时间。你希望为未来保留更多的选择，这样如果情况发生变化，你就能有空间进行调整，重新定位自己，抓住最大的机会。

How do you know when it's finally time to act?

> 你什么时候才能确定该采取行动了？

<span id="page-155-1"></span>When the cost of failure is cheap, the speed at which you come to a decision matters as much as the decision itself. When failure is expensive, it makes sense to learn more before taking action.

> 当失败的成本较低时，做出决策的速度与决策本身同样重要。而当失败的成本很高时，采取行动前则应先获取更多知识。

Defaults can transform caution into an excuse not to act if you don't resist them. Anyone who has held on to a failing job, relationship, or investment too long knows that information gathering reaches a point of diminishing returns—at some point the cost of getting more information is exceeded by the cost of losing time or opportunity.

> 默认设置可能会将谨慎转变为不采取行动的借口，如果你不去抵抗它们的话。任何曾经长时间固执地留在一个失败的工作、关系或投资中的人，都知道信息收集会达到边际效益递减的临界点——在某个时候，获取更多信息的成本将超过失去时间和机会的成本。

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> "defaults can transform caution into an excuse not to act" 翻译为“默认设置可能会将谨慎转变为不采取行动的借口”，意在保留原文中关于默认设置可能带来的消极影响的含义。

![](IMG-20260408090246254.jpeg)

A friend of mine works with engineers. He says they tend to be highly risk averse: they wait as long as possible to decide and can't tell when they should act more quickly. "They keep thinking that gathering more data will make things firmer," he said, "but they've already been prototyping and gathering information for months. They don't know when to stop and commit. They start losing interest in the problem too, because all they're doing is having meetings, aligning, gathering information, and writing up a giant document laying out how they made their decision. They're all aware of basic decision-making skills, but they really, really struggle to know when enough is enough." And it's not just engineers.

> 我的一位朋友和工程师们一起工作。他说，这些工程师通常非常规避风险：他们总是尽可能拖延做决定的时间，也难以判断何时应该迅速行动。“他们一直认为收集更多数据会让决策更加坚实，”他说，“但他们已经为原型设计和收集信息忙了数月。他们不知道何时该停止并做出承诺。他们也开始对问题本身失去兴趣，因为他们所做的只是开会、协调、收集信息，并撰写一份详尽的文件，说明他们是如何做出决定的。他们都知道一些基本的决策技巧，但真正困难的是，他们很难判断什么时候已经足够了。”这不仅仅是工程师的问题。

<span id="page-155-0"></span>Decision-makers at large have become increasingly susceptible to

> 大型决策者越来越容易受到影响。

analysis paralysis because so much data has become available to them. If you've ever struggled with analysis paralysis, a third principle can help you know when to stop deliberating and start acting:

> 由于可获取的数据量变得如此庞大，分析瘫痪现象也随之而来。如果你曾经经历过分析瘫痪，第三个原则将帮助你判断何时该停止反复思虑，开始行动：

**the stop, flop, know principle:** Stop gathering more information and execute your decision when either you Stop gathering useful information, you First Lose an OPportunity (FLOP), or you come to Know something that makes it evident what option you should choose.

> “停止、失败、知晓”原则：当你遇到以下三种情况之一时，就应停止继续收集信息并执行你的决定：你停止获取有用的信息；你失去了一个关键的机会（失败）；或者你获得了足够的信息，清楚地知道应该选择哪个选项。

Let's consider the Stop, FLOP, and Know conditions one at a time.

> 让我们逐一分析停止、FLOP 和了解这三种情况。

<span id="page-156-0"></span>First, when you've stopped gathering useful information, it's time to act. More information isn't always better, and there are signs that you've gathered enough. When I interviewed *The Princeton Review* co-founder Adam Robinson,[\[2\]](#page-210-0) for instance, he told me about a seminal study done by a psychologist named Paul Slovic back in 1974 that illustrates the folly of gathering too much information.

> 首先，当你已经停止收集有用的信息时，就是采取行动的时候了。更多的信息并不总是更好，而且有一些迹象表明你已经收集得足够多了。例如，当我采访《普林斯顿评论》联合创始人亚当·罗宾逊时，他告诉我，有一位名叫保罗·斯洛维奇的心理学家在1974年进行的一项具有开创性的研究，就说明了收集过多信息的弊端。

Slovic put eight horse handicappers in the same room and told them he wanted to see how well they could predict the winners of forty horse races, over four rounds of ten races each. In the first round each handicapper was given any five pieces of information he wanted on each horse. One handicapper might want the height and weight of the jockey; another might want the highest finish a horse had ever had. The handicappers also had to state how confident they were in their predictions.

> 斯洛维奇把八位赛马投注专家放在同一个房间，告诉他们自己想看看他们能多好地预测出四十场比赛的赢家，比赛分为四轮，每轮十场比赛。在第一轮中，每位投注专家可以自由选择每匹马的任意五条信息。一位专家可能想了解骑师的身高和体重，另一位可能更关注一匹马曾经取得过的最佳成绩。投注专家们还必须说明自己对预测结果的信心程度。

<span id="page-156-1"></span>At the end of the first round, with only five pieces of information, they were 17 percent accurate. Given there were ten horses to a race, they were 70 percent better than the 10 percent chance they'd have with zero information. They were 19 percent confident in their predictions, which isn't too far from their actual results.

> 第一轮结束时，他们仅凭五条信息，准确率达到了17%。考虑到每场比赛有十匹马，他们在没有信息的情况下只有10%的胜算，因此他们的表现比这好70%。他们对自己预测的置信度为19%，这与实际结果相差不远。

Each round gave them more and more information. For round two they were given ten pieces of information, then twenty in round three, and forty in the fourth and final round.

> 每一轮他们都获得了越来越多的信息。第二轮他们获得了十条信息，第三轮则是二十条，第四轮也是最后一轮则有四十条信息。

In the final round, they were still only 17 percent accurate. The

> 最后一轮比赛时，他们的命中率仍然只有17%。

thirty-five additional pieces of information did, however, move their confidence level to 34 percent. All of the extra information made them no more accurate but a lot more confident.

> 然而，另外还有35条信息使他们的信心水平提升到了34%。所有这些额外的信息并没有让他们更加准确，但却让他们更加自信。

Confidence increases faster than accuracy. "The trouble with too much information," Robinson told me, "is you can't reason with it." It only feeds confirmation bias. We ignore additional information that doesn't agree with our assessment, and gain confidence from additional information that does.

> 信心的增长速度比准确性更快。“过多信息的问题在于，”罗宾逊对我说，“你无法与之进行理性对话。”它只会助长确认偏误。我们忽视与自身判断相左的额外信息，而从与自身判断一致的额外信息中获得信心。

In my life and in the lives of people I've worked with, these are some signs you've hit the limit of useful information you can gather:

> 在我的生活中，以及我所接触过的人们的生命中，以下是一些表明你已经获取到有用信息的极限的迹象：

- You are able to argue credibly for and against the options you're considering from all angles.
- You're stretching for insight by asking people for advice who are more than one step removed from the problem or who don't have experience solving problems of this sort.
- You feel like you need to learn more, but you've stopped learning new things, and are instead in a constant loop reviewing the same information (or same arguments) over and over.

> 你可以从多个角度有力地论证你正在考虑的各种选择的优劣。  
你在寻求建议时，求助的人往往与问题相隔甚远，或者缺乏解决类似问题的经验，这种做法很难获得真正的洞察。  
你感觉需要学习更多，但事实上你已经停止了学习新内容，而是一直在重复地回顾同样的信息（或同样的论点）。  

>[!note]- 翻译注释  
>“stretching for insight” 翻译为“寻求真正的洞察”，意指努力获取深层次的理解，而非表面信息。  
>“constant loop reviewing the same information” 翻译为“一直在重复地回顾同样的信息”，意指陷入重复学习的循环中，无法突破。

<span id="page-157-0"></span>When you've hit any of these points, you've probably gotten all the useful information you're going to get. It's time to decide. That's Stop, now let's move on to FLOP.

> 如果你已经达到了上述任何一个要点，那么你可能已经获取了所有有用的信息。现在是时候做出决定了。停下吧，现在让我们进入FLOP阶段。

If you're facing a highly consequential and irreversible decision, and you're waiting as long as possible to make up your mind, the time to decide is when you start losing opportunities. For example, if you're selling a house, you might want to wait as long as possible to actually sell it. You go as far as listing it, setting a price, and getting offers, but when buyers start walking away, or you're about to break a legal contract, then you're starting to lose options, and it's time to act.

> 如果你正面临一个后果严重且不可逆的决定，而你一直在拖延做决定的时间，那么你应该在开始失去机会的时候做出决定。例如，如果你要出售一套房子，你可能希望尽可能长时间地等待出售。你可能会把房子挂牌出售，设定价格，甚至收到一些报价，但当买家开始放弃，或者你即将违反法律合同，那么你已经开始失去选择的机会，这时候就该采取行动了。

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> "breaking a legal contract" 翻译为“违反法律合同”以准确传达原意，同时保持自然流畅。

Likewise, suppose your partner wants to take your relationship to the next level—be it going exclusive, living together, or getting engaged. Those are big defining moments in your relationship, and if you're unsure, it makes sense to take your time deciding. But eventually your partner is going to get fed up and walk away. Right before that happens, when your partner makes it clear that you're on the threshold of losing options, it's time to decide.

> 同样，假设你的伴侣希望把你们的关系提升到一个新层次——无论是变得专一、同居，还是订婚。这些都是你们关系中的重大转折点，如果你拿不定主意，不妨花点时间慢慢决定。但最终，你的伴侣会感到厌倦并离开你。就在那之前，当你的伴侣明确表示你正站在失去选择的边缘时，就是你该做决定的时候了。

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> "fed up" 翻译为“感到厌倦”更符合中文表达习惯，传达出伴侣因等待而产生的失望情绪。"on the threshold of losing options" 翻译为“站在失去选择的边缘”保留原意，同时符合中文语境。

Remember, the rationale behind the ALAP Principle is to preserve optionality. When options start diminishing, it's time to act using whatever information you have. That's FLOP: if you're waiting to decide, wait no longer than your First Lost Opportunity.

> 记住，ALAP原则的初衷是为了保持选择的灵活性。当选择开始减少时，就要利用你所掌握的信息采取行动。这就是FLOP：如果你在等待做决定，那么不要等待超过你第一次失去的机会。

It's finally time to act when you come to know something that makes it clear what you should do. Sometimes you gather a critical piece of information that makes your decision easy, perhaps a First Lost Opportunity. Other times, especially in more ambiguous situations like relationships, it's just a gut feeling that doesn't go away or change. Either way, there's always a moment when you simply know at a core level exactly what to do.

> 当你了解到某些事情，清楚地知道该采取什么行动时，就是行动的时刻了。有时你会获得一个关键信息，使你的决定变得简单，也许是一个“首次错失的机会”。但有时，尤其是在关系等更模糊的情况下，你只是凭直觉行事，这种感觉不会消失或改变。无论如何，总有一个时刻，你会在内心深处清楚地知道自己该做什么。

>[!note]- 翻译注释  
>“First Lost Opportunity” 翻译为“首次错失的机会”，意指第一次错过的机会，常用于描述错过重要时机的情境。

Knowing what to do isn't enough, though. You have to take action.

> 知道该做什么还不够，你必须采取行动。

Do it!

> 做吧！

### 0.3 **CHAPTER 4.5**

> 4.5 章节

# 32 **Margin of Safety**

> 31 安全边际

<span id="page-159-0"></span>You don't always need to have the ultimate solution to make progress. If it remains unclear which path is best, often the next best step is just to eliminate paths that lead to outcomes you don't want. Avoiding the worst outcomes maintains optionality and keeps you moving forward.

> 你并不总是需要拥有终极解决方案才能取得进展。如果还不清楚哪条路最好，通常情况下，下一步最佳的做法就是剔除那些会导致你不想要结果的路径。避免最坏的结果可以保持选择的余地，并让你继续前进。

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> "Maintains optionality" 翻译为“保持选择的余地”更符合中文表达习惯，意指保留多种选择的可能性。

Sometimes things fail for reasons beyond our control. A lot of tricky and highly consequential decisions, though, fail for preventable reasons. When we don't consider how things might go wrong and plan for them in advance, we're left flat-footed when they do go wrong. Then we end up reacting instead of reasoning. It's much easier to plan for things that could go wrong in advance when you're calm and open-minded than it is to respond when things are in the midst of going wrong.

> 有时候事情失败的原因超出了我们的控制范围。然而，很多复杂且影响深远的决策失败，往往是因为可以避免的原因。当我们没有考虑事情可能出错的方式，并提前做好准备时，一旦出现意外，我们就会措手不及，只能仓促应对，而非冷静分析。在事情尚未出错时，若我们保持冷静和开放的心态，提前规划可能出错的情况，要比在问题已经发生时仓促应对要容易得多。

When failure is expensive, it's worth investing in large margins of safety.

> 当失败的成本很高时，投入较大的安全边际是值得的。

If you're an investor, you've likely heard the story of Long-Term Capital Management (LTCM), a hedge fund founded in 1994 by a prominent investor who managed to get two Nobel laureates on his board. LTCM had a high risk portfolio that was highly acclaimed for its incredible returns—over 21 percent in its first year, then 43 percent in its second year, and 41 percent in its third year.

> 如果你是一位投资者，你可能已经听说过长期资本管理公司（LTCM）的故事。这是一家成立于1994年的对冲基金，由一位知名投资者创立，他成功地将两位诺贝尔奖得主纳入其董事会。LTCM 拥有一个高风险的投资组合，因其惊人的回报率而备受赞誉——第一年回报率超过21%，第二年达到43%，第三年更是高达41%。
