Imagine being an investor in this environment. You see this hedge fund taking off, and your friends are boasting of their success and urging you to join in on the bonanza. They tell you about the amazing people working there—people with incredibly high IQs, including two Nobel Prize winners, who are also experienced in their respective fields and who have invested substantial amounts of their own money.

> 想象一下，你身处这样的投资环境中。你看到这家对冲基金蓬勃发展，朋友们则炫耀着他们的成功，并催促你加入这场盛宴。他们向你描述那里令人惊叹的人才——拥有极高智商的人，其中包括两位诺贝尔奖得主，他们不仅在各自领域经验丰富，还投入了大量自己的资金。

You watch your friends double and then quadruple their investments. You start wondering whether you should invest everything as well. Your own portfolio is returning 8 to 12 percent year over year—good returns, but not 40 percent! Is the rest of the world going to get rich while you play it safe?

> 你看着朋友们的投资翻倍、再翻倍，开始怀疑自己是否也应该把所有资金投入进去。你自己的投资组合每年都能获得8%到12%的回报——这已经是不错的收益了，但远远达不到40%！难道世界其他地方的人都在变得富有，而你却还在保守投资吗？

Consider now two scenarios. In the first, you decide to follow your friends and invest everything you have in the fund. A few months later, Asia and Russia experience a financial crisis. That crisis together with LTCM's highly leveraged investments loses them \$4.6 billion in less than four months. The chart on the next page shows what that loss would look like if you had invested \$1,000 from the beginning in 1994. In this scenario, you (and your friends) end up in financial ruin.

> 现在考虑两种情景。第一种情景是，你决定跟随朋友，将自己所有的资金都投入该基金。几个月后，亚洲和俄罗斯爆发了金融危机。这场危机加上长期资本管理公司（LTCM）高度杠杆化的投资，使他们在1994年刚开始时就损失了10亿美元。在这种情景下，你（以及你的朋友）最终会陷入财务崩溃的境地。

>[!note]- 翻译注释  
> LTCM 是“长期资本管理公司”（Long-Term Capital Management）的缩写，是一家著名的对冲基金，1998年因过度杠杆化投资而遭遇重大亏损，成为金融史上著名的案例。

Imagine now a different scenario. It's November 1997. You just hit the peak of returns at LTCM. If you anticipate that the future is going to be different from the past, you probably won't assume astronomical losses, and might invest a little. But if you're wise, you'll maintain a margin of safety.

> 现在想象一个不同的场景。1997年11月，你刚刚经历了LTCM的收益高峰。如果你预感到未来将不同于过去，你可能不会预计出现天文数字般的损失，也许会稍微投资一点。但如果你明智，你仍会保持一定的安全边际。

A margin of safety is a buffer between what you expect to happen and what could happen. It's designed to save you when surprises are expensive.

> 安全边际是在你预期发生的事情和可能实际发生的事情之间的缓冲区。它的设计目的是在意外发生时保护你，避免造成重大损失。

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> "margin of safety" 译为“安全边际”，这是经济学和工程学中常用的术语，表示在预期与实际之间留出的缓冲空间，以应对不确定性。

![](IMG-20260408090249776.jpeg)

A margin of safety is like having insurance. If you know in advance you won't need to make a claim this year, it's a waste of money to buy insurance. The problem is that you don't know in what year you'll need to make a claim, so you buy it every year. It might seem like a waste of money in years when nothing happens, but it shows its real value in years when something does.

> 安全边际就像拥有保险。如果你提前知道今年不会需要理赔，那么买保险就是一笔浪费。问题在于你并不知道自己会在哪一年需要理赔，因此每年都必须购买保险。在那些没有发生任何事情的年份里，这似乎是一种浪费，但一旦发生意外，它就会体现出真正的价值。

<span id="page-161-1"></span>Building a margin of safety means giving yourself as much cushioning and coverage in the future as possible. It's a way of preparing yourself for the widest range of possible future outcomes —and protecting yourself against the worst ones. In the second scenario, for instance, you can prepare yourself for the various bad outcomes that 1998 might bring by investing only one tenth of your portfolio in the fund. As a result, when the 1998 financial crisis comes, you'll lose at most 10 percent of your investments. That won't leave you happy, but it won't leave you financially ruined either.

> 建立安全边际意味着为你未来尽可能多地提供缓冲和保障。这是一种为未来可能出现的各种结果做好准备的方式——尤其是保护自己免受最坏结果的影响。例如，在第二种情景中，你可以通过只将投资组合的十分之一投入该基金，来为1998年可能带来的各种不利结果做好准备。因此，当1998年的金融危机到来时，你最多只会损失投资的10%。这不会让你感到快乐，但也不会使你陷入财务困境。

Your defaults are hard at work in the first scenario—not just the social default that convinces you that you're better off following the crowd, but the ego default as well. It convinces you that you don't need a margin of safety, because you know what's going to happen. You feel confident predicting the future—predicting that the future is going to be like the past, that LTCM's fourth year is going to be like its first three. The problem is that tomorrow is never exactly like today, and in that fourth year, the plan that garnered LTCM's success in the previous three years stops working.

> 你的默认设定在第一个情境中正发挥着作用——不仅仅是社会默认设定让你相信跟随人群对你更有利，还有自我中心的默认设定。它让你相信你不需要安全边际，因为你清楚事情将会如何发展。你自信地预测未来——认为未来会像过去一样，认为LTCM第四年会像前三年一样。问题在于，明天永远不会完全像今天，而到了第四年，之前三年让LTCM取得成功的计划就不再奏效了。

>[!note]- 翻译注释  
> "ego default" 翻译为“自我中心的默认设定”，意指人们倾向于依赖自我认知和自信，而非客观数据或谨慎策略。  
> "LTCM" 是“长期资本管理公司”（Long-Term Capital Management）的缩写，是一家著名的对冲基金，在1998年因俄罗斯债务危机导致巨额亏损。

In the second scenario, your decision isn't based on a prediction; it's instead prepared for a future in which your best-case scenario might not materialize. It's that preparation mindset—as opposed to a prediction mindset—that saves you in the second scenario.

> 第二种情况中，你的决定不是基于预测，而是为一个未来做准备，这个未来可能并不如你期望的那样理想。正是这种准备的心态，而非预测的心态，才能在第二种情况下救你一命。

<span id="page-161-0"></span>Warren Buffett has a saying that I often come back to: "Diversification is protection against ignorance. It makes little sense if you know what you are doing."[\[1\]](#page-210-1) The thing is, most of us rarely know what we are doing with the confidence required to go all in. When you don't know what you are doing, a margin of safety saves you from the worst outcomes. Even when you do know what you're doing and you make the best possible decision at the time, things can change.

> 沃伦·巴菲特有一句话我经常想起：“多元化是对无知的保护。如果你清楚自己在做什么，它就没什么意义。”事实上，大多数人很少能以足够的信心确定自己在做什么，从而敢于全身心投入。当你不清楚自己在做什么时，安全边际能帮你避免最糟糕的结果。即使你清楚自己在做什么，并且当时做出了最佳决策，情况也可能发生变化。

If the worst-case outcomes never come to pass, the margin of safety will appear like a waste. The minute you convince yourself you could have done better without a margin of safety is exactly when you need it most.

> 如果最坏的情况从未发生，安全边际看起来就像是浪费。一旦你说服自己，没有安全边际也能做得更好，那正是你需要它的时候。

We can't prepare for everything. Some horrific events defy imagination, and no amount of preparation can ever give you enough optionality to deal with them. Yet we know from history that there are certain unfortunate events that we're guaranteed to experience and that we can in fact prepare for, even if we have no idea when they'll arrive. On a personal level, these include:

> 我们无法为一切做好准备。一些可怕的事件超出了想象的范围，无论做多少准备，都无法提供足够的应对选择来应对这些事件。然而，从历史中我们知道，有一些不幸的事件是我们注定会经历的，而且我们实际上是可以提前做好准备的，即使我们不知道它们何时会发生。在个人层面，这些包括：

- Grief from losing a loved one
- Health issues
- Relationship changes
- Financial pressures
- Challenges in meeting our career goals

> 失去亲人的悲痛  
健康问题  
人际关系的变化  
经济压力  
在实现职业目标过程中遇到的困难  

>[!note]- 翻译注释  
> "Challenges in meeting our career goals" 翻译为“在实现职业目标过程中遇到的困难”，以更符合中文表达习惯。

On a more macro level, they include the following:

> 从更宏观的层面来看，它们包括以下内容：

- War and political dissent
- Natural disasters
- Environmental and ecological changes
- Economic fluctuations: both collapse and growth
- Technological advances and resistance to them

> 战争与政治异议  
自然灾害  
环境与生态变化  
经济波动：既包括崩溃也包括增长  
技术进步及其阻力  

>[!note]- 翻译注释  
> "Political dissent" 翻译为“政治异议”以保持原意，同时符合中文表达习惯。"Economic fluctuations" 翻译为“经济波动”更自然地传达了经济起伏的意思。

How do you build a margin of safety?

> 你如何建立安全边际？

Let's start with a very typical application. Engineers build margins of safety into everything they design. Suppose, for instance, that we're designing a bridge and calculate that on an average day it will need to support 5,000 tons at any one time.

> 让我们从一个非常典型的例子开始。工程师们会在他们设计的每一个项目中都加入安全余量。例如，假设我们正在设计一座桥，并计算出在普通的一天里，桥需要在任何时刻支撑5000吨的重量。

<span id="page-162-0"></span>If we build it to withstand 5,001 tons, we have no margin of safety: What if there's heavier traffic than usual one day? What if our calculations and estimates are a little off? What if the material weakens over time at a rate faster than we imagined? To account for all of these contingencies, we'd need to design the bridge to withstand 10,000 or even 20,000 tons. Why? Because we don't know what the future will bring. We don't know whether multiple trucks will ever get stuck on the bridge at the same time. We don't know whether vehicles in the future will be much heavier than they are now. We don't know many things about the future. So we design the bridge in a way that protects travelers in the widest range of possible future outcomes.

> 如果我们设计的桥梁能承受5,001吨的重量，那就没有安全余量了：如果有一天交通量比平常更重怎么办？如果我们的计算和预估稍有偏差怎么办？如果材料随时间推移比我们想象的更快老化或减弱怎么办？为了应对所有这些可能的情况，我们需要将桥梁设计成能承受10,000吨甚至20,000吨的重量。为什么？因为我们不知道未来会带来什么。我们不知道是否会有数辆卡车同时卡在桥上。我们不知道未来车辆是否会比现在重得多。我们对未来还有很多未知数。因此，我们以最广泛可能的未来结果为前提来设计桥梁，以保护所有可能的使用者。

Keep in mind as you're preparing for the future that the worst outcomes in history have always surprised people at the time. You can't use the historical worst case as your baseline. Engineers don't rely just on the historical use of current bridges. You have to really stretch your imagination to explore and anticipate what could potentially go wrong.

> 在为未来做准备时，请记住历史上最糟糕的结果在发生时总是出乎人们的意料。你不能将历史上的最坏情况作为你的基准。工程师们并不只是依赖当前桥梁的历史使用情况。你必须真正发挥想象力，去探索和预见到什么可能会出问题。

Here's a simple heuristic for creating a margin of safety so you know when "enough is enough."

> 这里有一个简单的启发式方法，用于创建一个安全边际，以便你知道何时“已经足够了”。

**tip:** The margin of safety is often sufficient when it can absorb double the worst-case scenario. So the baseline for a margin of safety is one that could withstand twice the amount of problems that would cause a crisis, or maintain twice the amount of resources needed to rebuild after a crisis.

> 提示：当安全边际能够吸收最坏情况的两倍时，通常就已经足够了。因此，安全边际的基础应是能够承受引发危机的两倍问题，或在危机后维持两倍的资源以进行重建。

For example, if you want to feel financially secure even if you lose your job, you can estimate how long it will take you to gain employment again, and then save enough to live off savings for double that amount of time.

> 例如，如果你想在失去工作的情况下依然感到经济上安全，可以先估算自己重新找到工作的大概时间，然后存够足够的钱，以维持这段时间的两倍时间的生活开销。

<span id="page-163-0"></span>That's our baseline. But we need to adapt our margin of safety to the individual and situational circumstances. If the cost of failure is high, and outcomes are more consequential, you want a large margin of safety. For instance, if you're worried about losing your job, and you're in a sector or economy that's volatile, you'll want to increase the length of time you can take care of yourself while unemployed.

> 这就是我们的基准。但我们需要根据个人和具体情境调整安全边际。如果失败的成本很高，结果更为重要，那么你就需要更大的安全边际。例如，如果你担心失去工作，而你所处的行业或经济环境波动较大，那么你就需要增加在失业期间能够维持自身生活的时间长度。

If the cost of failure is low, and outcomes are less consequential, you can often reduce or skip the margin of safety. The longer something exists and performs well, the higher the probability that its pattern of success will continue. Coca-Cola isn't going anywhere in the near future; neither is Johnson & Johnson.

> 如果失败的成本很低，且结果的影响不大，你通常可以减少甚至省略安全边际。某样东西存在并表现良好越久，其成功模式持续下去的概率就越高。可口可乐在可预见的将来不会消失；强生公司也是如此。

Yet even established patterns aren't foolproof. As Nassim Taleb writes in *The Black Swan*, "Consider a turkey that is fed every day. Every single feeding will firm up the bird's belief that it is the general rule of life to be fed every day by friendly members of the human race 'looking out for its best interests,' as a politician would say. On the afternoon of the Wednesday before Thanksgiving, something unexpected will happen to the turkey. It will incur a revision of belief." Our outcomes can sometimes upend even our most well-established expectations.

> 然而，即便是我们习以为常的模式也未必可靠。正如纳西姆·塔勒布在《黑天鹅》中所写：“想象一只每天都被喂食的火鸡。每一次喂食都会强化这只火鸡的信念，即它一生中每天都会被友善的人类成员喂养，这些人‘关心它的最佳利益’，就像政治家所说的那样。然而，在感恩节前的周三下午，对这只火鸡来说，一件意想不到的事情将会发生。它将不得不重新评估自己的信念。” 我们的结果有时甚至会颠覆我们最根深蒂固的预期。

>[!note]- 翻译注释  
>“turkey”在这里是比喻，翻译为“火鸡”以保留原意，同时让中文读者更容易理解其象征意义。

However, if you have a lot of expertise and data, you can reduce your margin of safety yet further. Here's an example: Warren Buffett aims to buy stocks that are 30–50 percent less than their true value. So he has a 30–50 percent margin of safety on stocks. But he'll pay close to a dollar on the dollar for stocks that he understands well. So there's only maybe a 20 percent margin of safety on the stocks he's the most confident in.

> 然而，如果你拥有大量的专业知识和数据，就可以进一步降低安全边际。举个例子：沃伦·巴菲特希望购买价格比其内在价值低30%至50%的股票。因此，他所持股票的安全边际是30%至50%。但他会对那些他非常了解的股票按面值几乎全额购买。因此，对他最放心的股票来说，安全边际可能只有20%左右。

<span id="page-164-0"></span>One of Warren Buffett's core tenets for buying a business is that if he doesn't understand it, he doesn't buy it. In other words, if he doesn't have enough information to calculate a margin of safety, he doesn't invest at all. He also knows that not all margins of safety will protect him—the goal isn't to get it perfect for every stock he buys; it's to use the best possible strategy for all his stocks in the big picture.

> 沃伦·巴菲特在购买企业时的核心原则之一是，如果他不理解一家公司，他就不会购买它。换句话说，如果他没有足够的信息来计算安全边际，他就完全不会投资。他也明白，并非所有的安全边际都能保护他——目标不是让每只股票都完美无缺，而是从整体上为他所持有的所有股票使用最佳的投资策略。

Here's the bottom line: Predicting the future is harder than it seems. Things are great until they're not. If things are good, a margin of safety seems like a waste. When things go wrong, though, you can't live without it. You need a margin of safety most at the very moment you start to think you don't.

> 这才是重点：预测未来比看起来要困难得多。一切看似美好，直到事情变糟。当一切顺利时，安全边际似乎是一种浪费。但一旦事情出错，你就离不开它。你最需要安全边际的时刻，正是你开始以为自己不需要它的时刻。

#### 0.1.1 **Bullets before Cannonballs**

> 31.1.1.1 子弹先于炮弹

If you're still gathering information, don't get overinvested in just one option. Keep your future options open by taking small, low-risk steps toward as many options as possible before committing everything to just one.

> 如果你还在收集信息，不要过度投入于某一个选项。在完全投入之前，应尽量采取小而低风险的步骤，探索尽可能多的选择，以保持未来的可能性。

When you're gathering information about your options, your

> 当你在收集有关选项的信息时，你的

<span id="page-165-1"></span>best bet is to gather as much information as possible about each without investing too much time, money, or energy in any particular one. In *Great by Choice*, Morten Hansen and Jim Collins call this approach shooting bullets before cannonballs:[\[2\]](#page-210-3)

> 最好的办法是尽可能多地收集每个选项的信息，但不要在任何一个选项上投入过多的时间、金钱或精力。在《伟大而非完美》一书中，莫滕·汉森（Morten Hansen）和吉姆·柯林斯（Jim Collins）将这种做法称为“先开枪，再放大炮”：[2]（第210页）

Picture yourself at sea, a hostile ship bearing down on you. You have a limited amount of gunpowder. You take all your gunpowder and use it to fire a big cannonball. The cannonball flies out over the ocean . . . and misses the target, off by 40 degrees. You turn to your stockpile and discover that you're out of gunpowder. You die.

> 想象一下你在海上，一艘敌船正向你逼近。你只有一小份火药。你把所有的火药都用尽，发射出一颗大炮弹。炮弹飞过海洋……却偏离了目标，偏了40度。你转向你的火药储备，却发现已经用完了火药。你死了。

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> “stockpile”在此处意为“储备”或“库存”，根据上下文译为“火药储备”更符合中文表达习惯。

But suppose instead that when you see the ship bearing down, you take a little bit of gunpowder and fire a bullet. It misses by 40 degrees. You make another bullet and fire. It misses by 30 degrees. You make a third bullet and fire, missing by only 10 degrees. The next bullet hits—ping!—the hull of the oncoming ship. Now, you take all the remaining gunpowder and fire a big cannonball along the same line of sight, which sinks the enemy ship. You live.[\[3\]](#page-210-2)

> 但假设你看到敌船正朝你驶来，你取出一点火药，发射一颗子弹。这颗子弹偏离了40度。你再发射一颗，偏离了30度。你第三次发射，只偏离了10度。第四颗子弹击中了——砰！——敌船的船身。此时，你把剩下的所有火药都用上，沿着同样的视线方向发射一颗大炮弹，敌船便沉没了。你活了下来。[[3]](#page-210-2)

<span id="page-165-0"></span>Here's an example of bullets before cannonballs that I witnessed in real life. A client of mine—we'll call him Solomon—was looking to hire someone to run his manufacturing business so he could step down and pursue other opportunities. He tried twice to choose a CEO to replace him. But each time, while the candidate looked great on paper, they didn't work out in practice.

> 我曾亲眼目睹过一个真实案例，体现了“先有子弹，后有炮弹”的现象。我的一位客户——我们称他为所罗门——想要聘请一个人来经营他的制造企业，这样他就可以退休并追求其他机会。他尝试了两次挑选继任的CEO，但每次候选人都在纸上看起来非常优秀，实际操作中却并不奏效。

I recommended that instead of investing heavily in one candidate and turning down others, he should have two or three candidates perform a small test project lasting a couple weeks. These small simultaneous tests would maintain his optionality, and seeing the candidates perform in real life would be magnitudes more insightful than interviewing them or reading their résumés.

> 我建议他不要把大量资源投入到一个候选人身上而拒绝其他候选人，而是应该让两个或三个候选人执行一个为期几周的小型测试项目。这些小型同时进行的测试可以保持他的选择余地，而观察候选人在真实环境中的表现，将比面试他们或阅读他们的简历更加具有洞察力。

The two candidates were paid well for their time and tasked with a project that required them to work with the team to understand the problem, gather information, and chart a course forward.

> 两位候选人因他们的工作时间得到了丰厚的报酬，并被指派了一个项目，要求他们与团队合作，理解问题、收集信息，并规划前进的方向。

The plan worked, and it yielded a surprising result: the candidate

> 计划成功了，而且结果出人意料：候选人竟然……

with the less impressive résumé was far and away the best with the team and made recommendations that ended up saving Solomon's company more than they'd paid for the project. More importantly, if neither of the candidates had worked out, the company wasn't saddled with an expensive exit cost.

> 简历不那么出色的那个人在团队中表现远胜于另一位，而且他提出的建议最终帮索洛蒙的公司节省的费用超过了项目本身的支出。更重要的是，如果这两位候选人中任何一位都不合适，公司也不会承担高昂的退出成本。

<span id="page-166-0"></span>Performing small, low-risk experiments on multiple options—in other words, shooting bullets and calibrating—keeps your options open before you commit the bulk of your resources to shooting a cannonball. Thinking about medical school? Shadow a doctor or a resident for a day. Take the MCAT and see what you score, or apply to colleges and see where you're accepted. Thinking about a new career? Try doing it freelance a few nights per week first. Thinking about launching a new product? See if people are willing to pay for it before you build it.

> 在多个选项上进行小规模、低风险的实验——换句话说，就是先发射子弹进行校准——这样在你投入大量资源去发射一枚炮弹之前，仍能保留更多选择。如果你在考虑读医学院，可以花一天时间跟随一位医生或住院医师观察；参加MCAT考试，看看自己能考多少分，或者申请大学，看看自己能被录取到哪里。如果你在考虑换一种新职业，不妨先尝试每周做几次自由职业工作；如果你在考虑推出新产品，最好先看看人们是否愿意为此付费，再开始制作。

Preserving options carries a cost and can make you feel like you're missing out. It's hard watching others take action sometimes, even when those actions don't make sense for you. Don't be fooled! This is the social default at work. It tempts you to feel like it's okay to fail so long as you're part of the crowd.

> 保留选择会带来代价，让你感觉好像错过了什么。有时候看着别人采取行动，即使这些行动对你并不合适，也会让人感到很难受。不要被迷惑了！这正是社会默认模式在起作用。它会诱使你觉得自己失败也没关系，只要你是群体中的一员。

While some people are quick to join the crowd, others prefer to be correct. Preserving optionality can make you look stupid in the short term, which means that from time to time you'll have to tolerate people treating you like you're a fool. But if you look at the most successful people in the world, they've all looked short-term stupid on a number of occasions, when they were keeping their options open and waiting for the right time to act.

> 有些人总是急着随大流，而另一些人则更倾向于做正确的事。保持选择的余地可能会在短期内让你显得愚蠢，这意味着你有时必须忍受别人把你当作傻瓜来对待。但如果你观察世界上那些最成功的人，他们都在很多场合下为了保持选择的余地、等待合适的时机行动而显得短期“愚蠢”。

Warren Buffett sat out most of the dot-com craze of the late '90s, and appeared to miss the stampeding bull market that came with it. People began chattering that he'd lost his touch. He may have looked stupid to some speculators for a few years—until the tech bubble burst and he still had tremendous cash reserves.

> 沃伦·巴菲特在20世纪90年代末的互联网泡沫时期大部分时间都置身事外，似乎错过了随之而来的牛市热潮。人们开始议论纷纷，说他失去了投资的敏锐度。在几年时间里，一些投机者可能觉得他显得有些笨拙——直到科技泡沫破裂，他依然拥有巨额的现金储备。

### 0.2 <span id="page-166-1"></span>**Live with a Decision before Announcing It**

> 31.1.2 在宣布决定之前先接受它

Have you ever spent time crafting an email, and then instantly regretted it as soon as you hit "Send"? I have. It's one of the worst feelings in the world. Perhaps not as bad, though, as announcing a major decision too soon and then realizing it was a mistake.

> 你有没有过这样的经历：花了不少时间写一封邮件，结果一点击“发送”就立刻后悔了？我也有过。这大概是世界上最糟糕的感觉之一。不过，也许比这更糟糕的是，过早地宣布一个重大决定，随后才意识到自己犯了错误。

Many leaders want to announce a decision the moment they've made it. This is natural: they want to show others how decisive they are, and let everyone else revel in their dazzling new venture. But announcing right away can be like the email you can't unsend. It starts things moving, and makes changing your mind much harder. That's why I created a rule for myself: I make major decisions and then sleep on them before telling anyone.[\[\\*\]](#page-284-0)

> 很多领导人都希望在做出决定的那一刻就立刻宣布。这是很自然的：他们想向别人展示自己多么果断，让其他人也能分享他们新计划的光彩。但立即宣布往往就像你发出去后无法撤回的电子邮件，它会启动一系列行动，让之后改变主意变得困难得多。因此，我为自己制定了一条规则：做出重要决定后，我会先静下心来好好思考，再告诉任何人。

<span id="page-167-1"></span>However, it turned out that sleeping on decisions by itself wasn't enough. I added another element to the rule: before going to bed, I would write a note to myself explaining why I'd made the decision. Doing so allowed me to make the invisible visible. When I woke up in the morning, I'd read the note. More often than I'd like to admit, my best thinking from the day before fell short upon inspection in the harsh light of the morning. Sometimes I'd realize I really didn't understand the problem as well as I thought I did. Other times, it just didn't feel right anymore. And I've come to learn that this feeling is important to explore.

> 然而，我发现仅仅靠睡觉来帮助做决定还不够。我为规则添加了另一个要素：在睡觉前，我会给自己写一张便条，解释我为什么做出那个决定。这样做让我能够将那些原本看不见的想法变得清晰可见。早上醒来后，我会再读一遍这张便条。比我愿意承认的要多得多，前一天我最好的想法在清晨的阳光下仔细审视时，往往显得力不从心。有时候，我会意识到自己其实并没有那么清楚地理解问题。其他时候，我则会感觉事情不再对劲。我渐渐明白，这种感觉值得深入探索。

<span id="page-167-2"></span><span id="page-167-0"></span>Living with a decision before announcing it allows you to look at it from a new perspective and verify your assumptions. Once you've made the decision—even if you haven't communicated it—you start seeing things in a new light.[\[\\*\]](#page-285-0) Your brain processes all the potential results of the decision as though it had already been made and put into action. This can often help you see nuances you might have missed, and those might, in turn, change how you implement the decision. Maybe you're promoting someone, and you're worried about their ability to lead a meeting and organize a team. Living with the decision might spur you to have them organize a meeting, see what happens, and recalibrate if necessary.

> 在宣布决定之前先让自己生活于这个决定之中，可以帮助你从新的角度审视它，并验证自己的假设。一旦你做出了决定——即使尚未告知他人——你开始以全新的视角看待事物。[[\*\]](#page-285-0) 你的大脑会像这个决定已经做出并付诸实施一样，处理所有可能的结果。这常常能帮助你发现一些之前可能忽略的细节，而这些细节又可能反过来改变你实施决定的方式。例如，你可能正在提拔某人，但担心他是否具备领导会议和组织团队的能力。在心中先接受这个决定，可能会促使你让他组织一次会议，观察实际情况，必要时再进行调整。

Also, living with a decision on your own for a day—or even two —allows you to check it with your emotions. Does this decision feel good in your bones? Do your brain and your heart and your guts all agree with it? Most decisions will feel fine. But a handful won't. If one doesn't feel right, it's a sign that something is off, and you need to dig deeper before announcing your decision. Keeping it to yourself before executing allows you to keep open the possibility of undoing it.

> 此外，独自生活一天——甚至两天——也能让你用情感来检验这个决定。这个决定是否让你感到舒服？你的大脑、内心和直觉是否都认同它？大多数决定都会感觉不错。但少数几项可能不会。如果其中一项感觉不对，那就说明有些地方出了问题，你得在宣布决定之前深入挖掘。在执行之前保持沉默，能让你保留撤销决定的可能性。

Implementing fail-safes will help ensure that your decision is executed according to plan.

> 实施安全措施将有助于确保您的决策按照计划执行。

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> "Fail-safes" 翻译为 “安全措施” 是指在系统或流程中设置的预防性机制，以确保在出现故障时仍能保持安全或按计划运行。

<span id="page-168-0"></span>Imagine standing on Mount Everest, just fifty meters from reaching the very top. Your entire body is aching. Your mind is numb. It feels like no matter how hard you breathe there's just not enough oxygen. You've been training for years, spent \$60,000 on guides and travel, sacrificing time with your family and friends in the process. You've told everyone today is the day you'll make the attempt. Everything you've worked for is right in front of you. You can see your goal. You're nearly there. But you're thirty minutes behind schedule, and oxygen is running low. Do you turn around or push forward?

> 想象一下，你正站在珠穆朗玛峰上，距离峰顶仅剩下五十米。你的全身都在疼痛，头脑麻木，感觉无论怎么呼吸，似乎都难以获得足够的氧气。你已经训练了好几年，花费了六万美元请向导和旅行，过程中也牺牲了与家人和朋友相处的时间。你曾告诉所有人，今天就是你尝试登顶的日子。你所付出的一切，此刻都近在咫尺。你能够看到目标，你几乎就要到达了。然而，你已经比计划时间晚了三十分钟，氧气也所剩无几。你是选择转身离开，还是继续前进？

The world's best Sherpas know that the most dangerous part of summiting Mount Everest isn't reaching the peak; it's the descent. So much energy is spent getting to the top that even if climbers are running out of strength or oxygen, they keep pushing themselves to the summit. They spend so much of their resources getting there, they neglect to account for the ordeal of getting back. Lost in "summit fever" they forget that the most important thing isn't making it to the top, but making it home. You can't win, after all, if you don't survive.

> 世界上最好的夏尔巴人知道，攀登珠穆朗玛峰最危险的部分不是抵达顶峰，而是下山。为了到达山顶，人们耗费了大量体力，即使登山者已经力竭气喘，他们仍会坚持把自己推上顶峰。他们把大部分精力都用在了到达山顶上，却忽视了返回的艰难。他们沉溺于“登顶热”，忘记了最重要的不是抵达顶峰，而是安全回家。毕竟，如果你连生命都保不住，就谈不上胜利。

For those of us outside the situation, likely with no plans to climb Everest, the idea of summit fever seems a bit ridiculous: reaching the summit isn't worth someone's life! But for those on the top of the mountain, turning their backs on a dream that's so close they can see it is much harder. Plus, the incredible amounts of energy spent climbing the mountain stresses the body and impairs the mind—conditions that the defaults use to subvert your carefully laid plans and prevent you from reaching your true objectives.

> 对于那些身处事外、很可能根本没有打算攀登珠穆朗玛峰的人来说，这种“登顶热”的想法似乎有些荒谬：到达山顶根本不值得用一个人的生命去换取！但对于那些已经站在山顶的人而言，转身放弃一个近在咫尺、甚至能够清晰看到的梦想，却要困难得多。此外，攀登过程中消耗的巨大能量会令身体疲惫，影响思维——而这些正是那些“默认设置”用来颠覆你精心制定的计划、阻止你实现真正目标的条件。

Climbing Everest is a dramatic example of why it's important to implement execution fail-safes to ensure your decision gets executed as planned. Is it finally time to bail when you're running out of oxygen? Should you stay the course even though your other equipment is on its last legs? Execution fail-safes leverage your thinking when you're at your best to protect you against the defaults when you're at your worst.

> 攀登珠穆朗玛峰是一个戏剧性的例子，说明了为何实施执行防故障机制非常重要，以确保你的决策能够按照计划执行。氧气即将耗尽时，是否终于该放弃了？即使其他装备也快用完了，你是否仍要坚持原计划？执行防故障机制能够在你思维最清晰的时候利用你的判断力，从而在你最脆弱的时候保护你免受默认反应的影响。

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> "execution fail-safes" 翻译为“执行防故障机制”，意指在执行过程中设置的自动保护机制，以防止出现严重问题。

<span id="page-169-0"></span>The idea of an execution fail-safe is well illustrated by the Greek myth of Ulysses. Ulysses was the captain of his ship. He and his crew were navigating close to the island inhabited by the Sirens, dangerous creatures that lured sailors to their deaths with their song—a song so beautiful that it drove crews mad with longing till they ran their ships against the rocks trying to reach its source.

> 执行失败保险机制的概念在希腊神话中乌利西斯的故事中得到了很好的体现。乌利西斯是自己船只的船长。他和他的船员们正在接近居住着塞壬的岛屿，这些塞壬是一种危险的生物，它们用歌声引诱水手走向死亡——那歌声如此动听，以至于船员们会因渴望而疯狂，最终驾驶船只撞向岩石，试图抵达歌声的源头。

Ulysses wanted to hear the Sirens' song without risking the lives of his crew. Now, I'm not saying that Ulysses made a great decision here. If he'd really thought through his options using the principles and safeguards I've outlined, he would've steered clear of the island. But that's not the part I love about the story. What I love is that Ulysses implemented fail-safes to ensure his decision was executed as planned.

> 奥德修斯想要在不危及船员生命的情况下听到塞壬的歌声。现在，我并不是说奥德修斯在这里做出了一个明智的决定。如果他真的按照我刚才提到的原则和保障措施来仔细考虑各种选择，他本应避开那座岛屿。但我不喜欢故事的这部分。我真正喜欢的是奥德修斯采取了各种防备措施，以确保他的计划能够按预期执行。

He stuffed the ears of his crew with beeswax so they couldn't hear the song as they approached the island. And to prevent them from changing course, he had them tie him to the mast so that no matter what he said or did in the madness of the song, he couldn't influence them or change the decision he'd already made. He also instructed them that the more he struggled and insisted on changing course, the tighter they should bind him.

> 他用蜂蜡堵住船员的耳朵，这样他们靠近岛屿时就听不见这首歌了。为了防止他们改变航向，他让他们把自己绑在桅杆上，无论他在歌曲带来的疯狂中说些什么或做出什么举动，都无法影响他们或改变他早已做出的决定。他还告诉他们，他挣扎得越厉害，坚持要改变航向，他们就应该绑得越紧。

Ulysses's clever implementation of execution fail-safes allowed him to hear the song while ensuring the safety of his crew. Of course, fail-safes are indispensable in a lot of other contexts too.

> 奥德修斯巧妙地运用了执行安全机制，使他能够听到歌声的同时确保船员的安全。当然，在许多其他情境下，安全机制也必不可少。

#### 0.2.1 **Three Kinds of Execution Fail-Safes**

> 31.1.2.1 三种执行防故障机制

There are three kinds of execution fail-safes you should know: setting trip wires, empowering others to make decisions, and tying your hands.

> 你应该了解三种执行防故障机制：设置触发线、授权他人做决定，以及自我束缚。

**fail-safe:** Set up trip wires to determine in advance what you'll do when you hit a specific quantifiable time, amount, or circumstance.

> 防故障机制：设置触发线，提前确定在遇到特定可量化的时间、数量或情况时将采取的行动。

<span id="page-169-1"></span>Trip wires are forms of precommitment—you commit yourself in advance to a course of action when certain conditions arise. For example, a team climbing Everest might set up a trip wire by committing themselves to abort their summit attempt if they don't reach a certain location by a certain time. If the team fails, they turn back! No argument. They don't try to decide in the midst of fatigue and oxygen deprivation; they've already decided and are already committed to turning around.

> “触发线”是一种预先承诺的形式——当你在某些条件出现时，提前承诺自己采取某种行动。例如，一支攀登珠穆朗玛峰的队伍可能会设置一条触发线，承诺如果在特定时间内未能到达某个地点，就放弃登顶计划。如果队伍失败，他们将立即返回！无需争论。他们不会在疲劳和缺氧的情况下试图做出决定；他们早已决定并承诺要返回。

The path to success and failure is marked if you know where to look. The journey always contains the answers. Trip wires include both negative signs and the absence of positive signs. When the signs are positive, you know to stay the course. When things are murkier, however, that's when it helps to set trip wires.

> 成功的道路与失败的道路都显而易见，只要你知道该去哪里寻找。这段旅程本身就包含了答案。警示信号包括负面标志以及缺乏正面标志的情况。当信号是正面的时，你便知道要继续坚持下去。然而，当情况变得模糊不清时，设置警示信号就显得尤为重要。

Negative signs are red flags that something is going seriously off course. The sooner you catch yourself going the wrong way, the easier it is to turn back. The other day I ended up going east on the highway when I wanted to go west. Only when I noticed the wrong city was getting closer did I realize my mistake! But negative signs aren't the only ones to take note of. Sometimes the absence of positive signs is itself a sign.

> 负向信号是事情严重偏离轨道的红色警示标志。你越早发现自己朝错误的方向前进，就越容易及时回头。前几天，我本想往西走，却误上了通往东边的高速公路。直到我注意到那个错误的城市越来越近时，才意识到自己的错误！但负向信号并不是唯一需要注意的。有时候，正向信号的缺失本身就是一个信号。

When you don't see the positive signs you expected, it doesn't necessarily mean things have gone wrong. It does mean this is a moment worth paying attention to. Many projects fail, and many decisions get challenging right at this point—when people see neither negative signs nor the positive ones they expected. When that happens, it's time to re-evaluate. Ask yourself, "Is the most important thing still the most important thing? Was I wrong? What will it take to reach my goals now that I've moved further in time but not in progress?"

> 当你没有看到预期的积极信号时，这并不一定意味着事情已经出了问题。这说明这是一个值得特别关注的时刻。许多项目会失败，许多决定在这一点上变得尤为困难——当人们既看不到负面信号，也看不到预期的正面信号时。当这种情况发生时，就是重新评估的时机。问问自己：“最重要的东西还是最重要的吗？我是不是错了？现在我已经在时间上向前走了，但进展却停滞不前，我需要付出什么才能实现目标？”

<span id="page-170-0"></span>By having clear trip wires in place before you start, you increase the odds of success. When the entire team understands clearly the markers of success and failure, they are empowered to act the minute things veer off course.

> 在开始之前设置明确的预警信号，可以提高成功的几率。当整个团队清楚地了解成功与失败的标志时，他们就能在事情偏离正轨的那一刻立即采取行动。

**fail-safe:** Use commander's intent to empower others to act and make decisions without you.

> 防故障机制：利用指挥官的意图来授权他人行动和做出决策，而无需你亲自参与。

Great leaders know that things don't always go according to plan.

> 优秀的领导者知道，事情并不总是按照计划进行。

They also know they can't be everywhere at once. Teams need to know how to adapt when circumstances change. And circumstances change all the time.

> 他们也清楚自己无法同时出现在所有地方。团队需要知道在情况发生变化时如何适应。而情况随时都在变化。

Giving a team enough structure to carry out a mission but enough flexibility to respond to changing circumstances is called *commander's intent*—a military term first applied to the Germans who were trying to defeat Napoleon.

> 给团队提供足够的结构以执行任务，同时又给予足够的灵活性以应对不断变化的情况，这被称为“指挥官意图”——这是一个最初用于描述德国人试图击败拿破仑时所采用的军事术语。

If you've ever been on the inside of a business where employees can't take action until everything is approved by their boss, you're seeing what happens without commander's intent. There's a single point of failure. If something happens to the boss, the business and mission fail.

> 如果你曾经在一家公司内部工作过，员工必须等到所有事情都得到老板批准才能采取行动，那么你正在看到缺乏指挥意图所带来的后果。这存在一个单一的故障点。如果老板遭遇不测，整个公司和任务都将失败。

Commander's intent empowers each person on a team to initiate and improvise as they're executing the plan. It stops you from being the bottleneck, and it enables the team to keep each other accountable to the goal without your presence.

> 指挥官的意图使团队中的每个人都能在执行计划时主动发起行动并灵活应变。它能防止你成为瓶颈，同时使团队在你不在场的情况下仍能相互监督，确保目标的实现。

<span id="page-171-0"></span>Commander's intent has four components: formulate, communicate, interpret, and implement. The first two components —formulate and communicate—are the responsibility of the senior commander. You must communicate the strategy, the rationale, and the operational limits to the team. Tell them not just what to do, but why to do it, how you arrived at your decision, so they understand the context, as well as the boundaries for effective action—what is completely off the table. Subordinate commanders then have the tools for the last two components: interpreting the changing contexts and implementing the strategy in those contexts.

> 指挥官意图包含四个组成部分：制定、传达、解释和执行。前两个组成部分——制定和传达——是高级指挥官的责任。你必须向团队传达战略、理由以及作战限制。告诉他们不仅要做什么，还要说明为什么这么做，以及你是如何做出决策的，这样他们才能理解背景以及有效行动的边界——即哪些事项完全不可行。下属指挥官则拥有执行后两个组成部分的工具：解释不断变化的背景，并在这些背景下实施战略。

Before you begin executing a decision, just so there's no confusion as you move forward, ask yourself:

> 在你开始执行决定之前，为了确保接下来的行动不会产生误解，请问自己：

- Who needs to know my goals and the outcomes I'm working toward?
- Do they know what the most important objective is?
- Do they know the positive and negative signs to look for and what trip wires are attached to them?

> 谁需要知道我的目标以及我正在努力实现的成果？  
他们是否知道最重要的目标是什么？  
他们是否了解应该关注哪些积极和消极的信号，以及这些信号所牵连的预警指标？  

>[!note]- 翻译注释  
>“trip wires”在此处翻译为“预警指标”，意指在达成目标过程中可能预示问题或转折的关键信号。

One sign that you've failed to empower your team is that you can't be away from the office for a week without things falling apart. Some leaders think it makes them indispensable—that the team's inability to function without them is a sign of how important they are. Don't be fooled! This is the ego default at work. Effective leaders shouldn't have to be available 24/7 for their team to make decisions and achieve objectives. If you can't be away, it doesn't mean that you're indispensable or a supremely competent leader; it means that you're an incompetent communicator.

> 一个表明你未能激励团队的迹象是，你离开办公室一周，事情就会变得一团糟。有些领导者认为这说明他们不可或缺——团队无法在他们不在时正常运作，这反而证明了他们的重要性。别被蒙蔽了！这正是自大的表现。真正有效的领导者不需要24小时在线，团队就能做出决策并达成目标。如果你无法离开，这并不意味着你不可或缺或是一个非常有能力的领导者，这意味着你是一个沟通能力极差的人。

Another sign that you're in the grips of the ego default is that you insist on controlling how everything happens. Good leaders determine what needs to get done and set the parameters for getting there. They don't care whether something gets done differently from how they themselves would've done it. As long as it advances to the objective within the limits they've set, they're satisfied.

> 另一个表明你陷入自我中心思维模式的迹象是，你坚持要控制一切事情如何发生。优秀的领导者会判断什么事情需要完成，并设定实现目标的参数。他们并不在意事情是否以与自己原本设想不同的方式完成。只要在他们设定的范围内，朝着目标前进，他们就会感到满意。

<span id="page-172-0"></span>Poor leaders insist that everything must be done their way, which ultimately demoralizes their team and undermines both loyalty and creativity—exactly the opposite of commander's intent.

> 平庸的领导者坚持一切都要按照自己的方式来做，这最终会使团队士气低落，并削弱忠诚度和创造力——这与指挥官的意图恰恰相反。

**fail-safe:** Tie your hands to keep your execution on track.

> 防故障机制：将双手绑住，确保执行不偏离轨道。

Ulysses used trip wires and commander's intent to safeguard his decision. He also had the crew tie his hands—a final execution failsafe to ensure he followed through with his decision, and the reason this kind of safeguard is known as a *Ulysses pact*.

> 奥德修斯使用了警戒线和指挥意图来保障自己的决定。他还让船员们绑住他的手——这是一种最终的执行保障措施，以确保他能够贯彻自己的决定，这也是为什么这种保障措施被称为“奥德修斯契约”的原因。

Tying your hands amounts to different things in different contexts. If you're dieting, tying your hands might mean ridding your home of all junk food so there's nothing to tempt you. If you're investing, it might mean creating automated deposits each month. If you're climbing Everest, tying your hands might mean securing an agreement from everyone that the team will turn around if they don't reach the halfway point by a certain time.

> 在不同情境下，“束缚双手”意味着不同的事情。如果你正在节食，“束缚双手”可能意味着彻底清理家中所有垃圾食品，以免诱惑你。如果你在投资，“束缚双手”可能意味着每月设置自动存款。如果你正在攀登珠穆朗玛峰，“束缚双手”可能意味着与团队所有人达成协议，如果在规定时间内未能到达半程点，团队将立即返回。

Whatever decision you're facing, ask yourself, "Is there a way to make sure I will stick to the path I've decided is best?" By thinking through your options, and precommitting to courses of action, you free up space to tackle other problems.

> 无论你面临什么样的决定，都问问自己：“有没有办法确保我坚持自己认为最好的选择？”通过思考你的各种选择，并预先承诺采取某种行动，你就能腾出空间去解决其他问题。

Even if we're waiting as long as possible to decide, we now know

> 即使我们尽可能长时间地等待做出决定，我们现在也知道了。

exactly what to focus on and do when the time comes to make our decision. We've set our trip wires, we've empowered people to act on them, and we've tied our hands so that we can't undo all our good work in a moment of stress.

> 到了做出决定的时候，我们清楚应该关注什么并采取什么行动。我们已经设定了预警信号，已经赋予人们采取行动的权力，还限制了自己的自由，以免在情绪激动的时刻毁掉我们之前的所有努力。

### 0.3 **CHAPTER 4.6**

> 4.6 章节

# 33 <span id="page-174-3"></span><span id="page-174-0"></span>**Learn from Your Decisions**

> 从你的决定中学习

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> "Learn from Your Decisions" 是一个标题，直译为“从你的决定中学习”，在中文中表达类似含义的短语自然流畅。

<span id="page-174-1"></span>If you're a knowledge worker, you produce decisions.[\[1\]](#page-210-4) That's your job. The quality of your decisions eventually determines how far you go and how fast you get there. If you learn to make great decisions consistently, you'll quickly move past the people whose decisions are merely good.

> 如果你是一名知识型工作者，你的工作就是做出决策。[[1]](#page-210-4) 你决策的质量最终将决定你能走多远以及能以多快的速度到达那里。如果你学会持续地做出卓越的决策，你将迅速超越那些仅仅做出良好决策的人。

No one is smart enough to make great decisions without learning first, though. Great decision-makers have mastered the ability to learn both from their mistakes and from their successes. It's that ability that sets them apart. It enables them to repeat their successes and avoid repeating their failures. Unless you develop that ability yourself, you won't improve your decision-making process over time.

> 没有人能在不先学习的情况下做出伟大的决定，尽管如此。伟大的决策者已经掌握了从自己的错误和成功中学习的能力。正是这种能力使他们脱颖而出。它使他们能够重复成功，避免重蹈覆辙。除非你自己培养这种能力，否则你的决策过程不会随着时间的推移而得到改善。

A few years ago, a firm engaged me to help them improve the quality of their decisions. As a first step, we needed to find out where they were at. We started by trying to answer a single question: When their decision-makers expected a particular result, how often did that result happen for the reasons they thought it would?

> 几年前，一家公司聘请我帮助他们提升决策质量。作为第一步，我们需要弄清楚他们目前所处的阶段。我们从回答一个问题开始：当决策者预期某种结果时，这种结果实际上发生的原因是否与他们所认为的一致？

<span id="page-174-2"></span>What we discovered shocked them: Their decision-makers were right only about 20 percent of the time. Most of the time when something that they anticipated actually happened, it didn't happen for the reasons they thought it would. Their success, in other words, wasn't due to insight or effort or skill. It was more luck than skill. This news was a blow to their egos. They thought that the successes they enjoyed resulted largely from their abilities, but the numbers told a different story. They were like people getting lucky at roulette and attributing their success to having a "system."

> 我们发现的结果让他们震惊：他们的决策者只有大约20%的时间是正确的。大多数情况下，他们预期的事情确实发生了，但并不是因为他们所认为的原因。换句话说，他们的成功并不是源于洞察力、努力或技能，而是运气多于技能。这个消息对他们自尊心是一个打击。他们以为自己取得的成功很大程度上源于自己的能力，但数据却讲述了一个不同的故事。他们就像在轮盘赌中运气好而误以为自己有“系统”的人。

The story illustrates a psychological phenomenon we've discussed before: *self-serving bias*, the tendency to evaluate things in ways that enhance our self-image. When we succeed at something, we tend to attribute our success to our ability or effort. By contrast, when we fail at something, we tend to attribute our failure to external factors. Basically, heads I'm right. Tails, I'm not wrong. If you want to get better, you have to rewrite the faulty narratives.

> 这个故事展示了一个我们之前讨论过的心理现象：自我服务偏差，即我们倾向于以增强自我形象的方式来看待事物。当我们成功时，往往会把成功归因于自己的能力或努力；相反，当我们失败时，却倾向于将失败归咎于外部因素。简而言之，抛硬币正面我没错，反面我也没错。如果你想有所进步，就必须重新编写这些错误的叙述。

Self-serving bias gets in the way of learning from your decisions and improving your process. Our ego default wants us to think that we're smarter than we are and tells us that we work harder and know more than we actually do. The overconfidence that the ego demon inspires prevents us from examining our decisions with a critical eye. It keeps us from distinguishing skill from luck—what's in our control from what isn't. If you get trapped by the demon, you'll never learn from your decisions and never get better at making them in the future.

> 自我服务偏见会妨碍我们从自己的决策中学习并改进流程。我们的自我意识默认倾向让我们以为自己比实际更聪明，告诉我们自己比实际更努力、知道得更多。自我意识恶魔带来的过度自信会阻止我们以批判的眼光审视自己的决策。它让我们无法区分技能与运气——哪些是我们能控制的，哪些是不能控制的。如果你被这个恶魔困住，你就永远无法从自己的决策中学习，也永远无法在未来做出更好的决策。

The first principle to keep in mind when evaluating your decisions is this:

> 做出决策时要牢记的第一条原则是：

**the process principle:** When you evaluate a decision, focus on the process you used to make the decision and not the outcome.

> 决策评估原则：在评估一个决定时，应关注你做出决定所使用的过程，而不是结果。

<span id="page-175-0"></span>Conventional wisdom suggests that good outcomes result from good people making good decisions and bad outcomes result from bad people making bad decisions. But it's easy to find counterexamples. We've all made bad decisions, yet we're not all bad people. And even good decisions can have unexpected and unfortunate outcomes thanks to the necessary uncertainty of life.

> 传统观念认为，好的结果源于好人做出的好决定，而坏的结果则源于坏人做出的坏决定。但要找到反例却并不困难。我们所有人都曾做出过糟糕的决定，却并不都是坏人。而且，即使是好的决定，也可能会因为生活中不可避免的不确定性而产生意外且不幸的结果。

Coach Pete Carroll of the Seattle Seahawks understands the difference between good decisions and good outcomes as well as anyone. In February 2015, Carroll made a historic call in the final minutes of Super Bowl XLIX that was immediately criticized as a huge mistake. The Seahawks trailed 28–24, but they were on New England's one-yard line and appeared certain to score and take the lead. Lined up in Seattle's backfield was Marshawn Lynch, a 215 pound battering ram who was arguably the most dominant running back in the NFL at the time, and who'd already run for more than one hundred yards against the Patriots that day. Here's a quick recap from a CBS Sports story that explains what happened next and how Carroll's decisions continue to be viewed today:

> 西雅图海鹰队的主教练佩特·卡罗尔深知好决策与好结果之间的区别，这一点无人能及。2015年2月，在超级碗XLIX的最后几分钟，卡罗尔做出了一次具有历史意义的决定，当时这一决定立刻遭到批评，被认为是巨大的失误。当时海鹰队以24比28落后，但他们已经来到新英格兰队的一码线，看起来即将得分并取得领先。在西雅图的进攻线后方，站着的是马肖恩·莱希，一名重达215磅的强力冲球手，当时他被认为是NFL中最具统治力的跑卫之一，而且当天他已经为海鹰队冲跑了超过100码。以下是来自CBS体育的一篇报道的简要回顾，解释了接下来发生了什么，以及卡罗尔的决定如今仍被如何看待：

What transpired next will live in the annals of this league for as long as football games are played . . . a bizarre call by Carroll to throw on second down on a route in the crowded middle of the field will be second-guessed just as long—[and resulted] in Belichick and Tom Brady, the game's MVP, making history with their fourth Super Bowl title together.[\[2\]](#page-210-5)

> 接下来发生的事情将被载入这个联盟的历史，只要足球比赛继续进行下去……卡罗尔在比赛拥挤的中场区域中，在第二次进攻时做出一个奇怪的判罚，让四分卫传球，这一决定将被反复质疑——[并且结果]比尔奇和汤姆·布雷迪，这场比赛的MVP，一起创造了历史，赢得了他们职业生涯的第四座超级碗冠军奖杯。[[2]](#page-210-5)

<span id="page-176-1"></span><span id="page-176-0"></span>To the fans in the stands and nearly everyone watching the game, the right decision seemed obvious: just hand the ball to "Beast Mode," as Lynch was known to many. But instead Carroll asked quarterback Russell Wilson to throw a pass, and the result was a disaster.

> 看台上的球迷和几乎所有观看比赛的人都认为，正确的选择似乎显而易见：只需把球交给“野兽模式”（Lynch 被许多人这样称呼）。但卡罗尔却让四分卫卢卡斯·威尔逊传球，结果却是一场灾难。

It's been years since this play. There's been a tremendous amount of analysis of it. Why didn't the coach make the easy choice that seemed so clear to everyone else? Based on good information, he was betting against his opponent's weaknesses. After the game, an interviewer told Carroll, "Everyone is thinking this was the greatest mistake of all time." Carroll's response: it was the "worst result of a call ever." His decision-making process was sound. It just didn't work out. Sometimes that's life.

> 这场戏剧已经上演多年，人们对它进行了大量分析。为什么教练没有做出看起来对所有人都非常明显的简单选择呢？根据可靠的信息，他是在押注对手的弱点。赛后，一位采访者告诉卡罗尔：“大家都认为这是有史以来最糟糕的失误。”卡罗尔的回应是：“这是有史以来最糟糕的判罚结果。”他的决策过程是合理的，只是结果没有如愿。有时候，这就是生活。

The right call doesn't always get the intended outcome. Sooner or later everyone who makes decisions in the real world learns this lesson. Poker players know it. They can play their hand perfectly and still lose. Nothing is guaranteed. All you can do is play the hand you're dealt as best you can.

> 正确的决策并不总能带来预期的结果。在现实世界中做决定的人，迟早都会明白这个道理。扑克玩家也懂得这一点。他们可以完美地打自己的牌，却仍有可能输掉。没有任何事情是绝对有保障的。你所能做的，就是尽你所能地打好你手中的牌。

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> "The right call" 指的是做出正确的决定或选择，这里根据上下文翻译为“正确的决策”；"play your hand" 是扑克术语，意指“打手中的牌”，这里译为“打好你手中的牌”以保持原意。

Carroll made his decision on the biggest stage in the world, and it had a terrible outcome. But his confidence in the decision was unwavering. Why? Because he knew the reasons why he had made the call. He knew his logic was sound. All he could do was learn from the outcome.

> 卡罗尔在世界最大的舞台上做出了决定，但结果却非常糟糕。然而，他对这个决定的信心始终未动摇。为什么？因为他清楚自己做出这个决定的原因。他知道自己逻辑严密。他唯一能做的，就是从结果中吸取教训。

Many people assume that good decisions get good outcomes and bad ones don't. But that's not true. The quality of a single decision isn't determined by the quality of the outcome. Here's a thought experiment that will help illuminate this concept.

> 很多人认为，好的决定会带来好的结果，而坏的决定则不会。但事实并非如此。一个决定的质量并不由其结果的质量决定。这里有一个思想实验，可以帮助你更好地理解这个概念。

Imagine you engage in a very thoughtful and intentional decision-making process concerning your career. You have offers from a few different companies, one being a startup and another a Fortune 500. Based on where you are in your life, you decide to go with the Fortune 500 company. The pay is less up front, but it appears to be more stable.

> 想象一下，你对自己的职业发展进行了非常认真且有意识的决策过程。你收到了几家不同公司的 offer，其中一家是初创公司，另一家则是财富 500 强企业。根据你目前的生活阶段，你决定选择加入这家财富 500 强企业。虽然起薪相对较低，但看起来更加稳定。

<span id="page-177-0"></span>Imagine your friend ends up working for the startup. You watch as he gets raises and more vacation time. Is your decision good or bad?

> 想象一下，你的朋友最终加入了一家初创公司。你看着他获得加薪和更多的带薪假期。你的决定是明智的还是错误的？

Now, imagine the startup quickly folds after only a year. Does this affect how you feel about your decision?

> 现在，想象一下这家初创公司只运营了一年就倒闭了。这会影响你对当初决定的看法吗？

I hope you get where I'm going with this. You can't control whether the startup takes off or not. Nor can you control in the moment how you feel about the startup offering higher pay. You can only control the process you use to make the decision. It's that process that determines whether a decision is good or bad. The quality of the outcome is a separate issue.

> 我希望你能明白我的意思。你无法控制创业公司是否会成功，也无法在那一刻控制你对创业公司提供更高薪资的感受。你唯一能控制的是做决定所使用的过程。正是这个过程决定了这个决定是好是坏。结果的质量则是另一个问题。

Our tendency to equate the quality of our decision with the outcome is called *resulting*. Results are the most visible part of a decision. Because of that, we tend to use them as an indicator of the decision's quality. If the results are what we wanted, we conclude that we made a good decision. If the results aren't what we wanted, we tend to blame external factors. It's not that our process was lacking; it's that a crucial bit of information was. (As opposed to when an acquaintance gets bad results, at which point we assume it's because they made a bad decision.)

> 我们倾向于将决策的质量与结果等同起来，这种倾向被称为“结果导向”。结果是一个决策中最显眼的部分。正因如此，我们往往用结果来判断决策的质量。如果结果符合我们的预期，我们就认为自己做出了正确的决策；如果结果不符合预期，我们则倾向于归咎于外部因素。并不是我们的过程有问题，而是缺少了关键的信息。（与当一位熟人取得糟糕结果时，我们则会假设是因为他做出了错误的决策不同。）

Obviously, we all want good outcomes, but as we've seen, good decisions can have bad outcomes, and bad decisions can have good ones. Evaluating decisions—ours or others'—based on the outcome (or how we feel about the outcome) fails to distinguish luck from skill and control. Because of that, engaging in resulting doesn't help us get better. The result of resulting is instead stagnation.

> 显然，我们都希望得到好的结果，但正如我们所看到的，好的决定可能会带来坏的结果，而坏的决定却可能产生好的结果。以结果（或我们对结果的感受）来评估决策（无论是自己的还是他人的），无法区分运气与技能以及控制力。因此，从事结果导向的行为并不能帮助我们变得更好。结果导向带来的反而是停滞不前。

If you've ever ruminated over a bad outcome—asking yourself again and again, "How did I not see that coming?"—then you've experienced how challenging and ultimately useless it is to judge your decisions on the basis of how you feel about them in retrospect. You think, "If only I had talked to that person (whom I didn't know at the time)!" or, "If I had only known that piece of information (which didn't exist at the time), I would have made the right choice." Even the best decision-makers get bad results from time to time, though.

> 如果你曾为一个不好的结果反复琢磨——不断问自己：“我怎么没早想到会这样？”——那你一定体会过，以事后的情绪来评判自己的决定是多么困难且最终毫无意义。你可能会想：“如果我当时能跟那个人（当时并不认识的人）谈谈就好了！”或者，“如果我当时知道那条信息（当时并不存在的信息），我就能做出正确的选择了。”即使是最优秀的决策者，也偶尔会得到不好的结果。

Making a good decision is about the process, not the outcome. One bad outcome doesn't make you a poor decision-maker any more than one good outcome makes you a genius. Unless you evaluate your reasoning at the time you made the decision, you'll never know whether you were correct or just lucky. Your reasoning at that time remains mostly invisible unless you take steps to make it visible.

> 做出一个好决定，关键在于过程，而非结果。一次糟糕的结果并不意味着你就是一个糟糕的决策者，就像一次成功的结果也不意味着你就是天才。除非你在做出决定时就评估自己的推理过程，否则你永远无法确定自己是对的还是仅仅运气好。你当时所做的推理，除非主动采取措施使其变得清晰可见，否则大多都是隐藏的。

Rarely are you making decisions that have a 100 percent chance of success. And the kind of decision that has a 90 percent chance of success still has a bad outcome 10 percent of the time. What matters are results over time and ensuring that 10 percent of the time won't kill you.

> 你很少做出百分之百成功的决定。即使是一个有90%成功率的决定，也仍然有10%的概率导致不好的结果。真正重要的是长期的结果，以及确保那10%的失败不会对你造成致命打击。

The following matrix provides a way of organizing your reflection on decisions and their outcomes.

> 以下矩阵为您提供了一种组织对决策及其结果进行反思的方式。

| Good<br>Bad<br>outcome<br>outcome                                                 |                                                                                                                                                                             |  |
|-----------------------------------------------------------------------------------|-----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|--|
| Good<br>You<br>make<br>process<br>a<br>good<br>decision<br>decision,              | and<br>but<br>things<br>go<br>don't<br>as<br>go<br>planned.<br>as<br>planned.<br>You<br>deserve<br>Bad<br>luck!<br>the<br>Don't<br>success<br>get                           |  |
| you<br>discouraged.<br>enjoy—you<br>Trust<br>earned<br>the<br>it.                 | process.Learn<br>Don't<br>let<br>it<br>go<br>from<br>to<br>your<br>theexperience<br>head.<br>Stay<br>on<br>and<br>track<br>continue<br>and<br>continue                      |  |
| improving.<br>improving<br>your<br>process.                                       |                                                                                                                                                                             |  |
| Bad<br>You<br>make<br>processa<br>bad<br>decision                                 | but<br>and<br>get<br>are<br>lucky—like<br>unlucky—like<br>winning<br>losing<br>at<br>at<br>roulette.<br>roulette.<br>Your<br>You<br>success<br>deserve<br>is                |  |
| undeserved.<br>failure.<br>You<br>earned<br>You<br>did<br>it.<br>nothing<br>Now   | learn<br>to<br>earn<br>from<br>it.<br>it.<br>You<br>Let<br>just<br>this<br>got<br>be<br>a<br>lucky.<br>wake-up<br>Eventually<br>call.<br>Change<br>you're<br>while<br>going |  |
| to<br>you<br>lose.<br>can.<br>Change<br>Grow<br>up<br>while<br>and<br>you<br>take | can.<br>command<br>Grow<br>up<br>ofyour<br>and<br>take<br>decision-making.<br>command<br>of<br>your<br>decision                                                             |  |
| making.                                                                           |                                                                                                                                                                             |  |
|                                                                                   |                                                                                                                                                                             |  |

> | 好<br>坏<br>结果<br>结果                                                 |                                                                                                                                                                             |  |
|-----------------------------------------------------------------------------------|-----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|--|
| 好<br>你<br>做<br>过程<br>一个<br>好<br>决定<br>决定，              | 并且<br>但<br>事情<br>发展<br>没<br>如<br>计划<br>进行。<br>如<br>计划<br>进行。<br>你<br>值得<br>坏<br>运气!<br>别<br>成功<br>得到                           |  |
| 你<br>受挫。<br>享受——你<br>信任<br>赢得<br>它。<br>它。                 | 过程。学习<br>别<br>让<br>它<br>从<br>你<br>头脑<br>中<br>溜走。<br>保持<br>专注<br>并<br>跟踪<br>继续<br>并<br>继续                      |  |
| 改进。<br>改进<br>你的<br>过程。                                       |                                                                                                                                                                             |  |
| 坏<br>你<br>做<br>过程<br>一个<br>坏<br>决定                                 | 但<br>并且<br>获得<br>幸运——像<br>不幸——像<br>赢得<br>输掉<br>在<br>在<br>轮盘赌。<br>轮盘赌。<br>你的<br>你<br>成功<br>值得<br>是                |  |
| 不值得。<br>失败。<br>你<br>赢得<br>你<br>做到<br>它。<br>什么都没<br>现在   | 学习<br>从<br>它<br>中<br>获得。<br>它。<br>你<br>让<br>只是<br>这<br>得到<br>成为<br>幸运。<br>醒来<br>最终<br>电话。<br>改变<br>你<br>在<br>前行 |  |
| 给你<br>你<br>输。<br>可以。<br>改变<br>成长<br>起来<br>同时<br>和<br>你<br>获取 | 可以。<br>掌控<br>成长<br>起来<br>你的<br>和<br>获取<br>决策能力。<br>掌控<br>你的<br>决策                                                             |  |
| 能力。                                                                           |                                                                                                                                                                             |  |

<span id="page-178-0"></span>A bad process can never produce a good decision. Sure, it might result in a good outcome, but that's different from making a good decision. Outcomes are influenced in part by luck—both good and bad. Getting the right result for the wrong reasons isn't a function of smarts or skills, but just blind luck.

> 一个糟糕的流程永远无法产生一个好的决策。当然，它可能会导致一个好的结果，但这是不同的概念。结果在一定程度上受到运气的影响——既有好的运气，也有坏的运气。因为错误的原因而得到正确的结果，并不是聪明或技能的体现，而只是盲目运气的产物。

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> “Getting the right result for the wrong reasons” 翻译为“因为错误的原因而得到正确的结果”，意指结果看似正确，但其背后的原因并不合理，这在中文中表达较为自然。

Don't get me wrong: it's nice to get lucky (provided you know it's luck). But luck isn't a repeatable process that secures good results over the long term. Luck isn't something you can learn, and it isn't something you can get better at. Luck won't give you an edge.

> 别误会我的意思：碰上好运（当然前提是你要意识到这是运气）的确很美好。但运气并不是一种可以长期重复并确保获得好结果的过程。运气不是你能学会的东西，也不是你能变得更好的东西。运气不会给你任何优势。

When you start equating luck with will, you're bound to make mistakes. You blind yourself to the risks you're taking, and are bound to be badly surprised sooner or later. And when you start confusing luck with skill, you're bound to squander opportunities to learn from your decisions, to improve your process, and to secure better results over the long term.

> 当你开始将运气等同于意志时，你注定会犯错误。你会忽视自己所承担的风险，最终迟早会受到严重打击。而当你开始混淆运气与技能时，你注定会浪费学习决策、改进流程、实现长期更好结果的机会。

A second principle for evaluating your decisions in retrospect is this:

> 评估你过去决定的第二个原则是：

**the transparency principle:** Make your decision-making process as visible and open to scrutiny as possible.

> 透明原则：尽可能让您的决策过程公开透明，便于监督。

Evaluating other people's decisions is different from evaluating our own. We rarely see other people's intentions, thinking, or process, so it's hard judging their decisions by reference to anything other than their outcomes.

> 评价他人的决定与评价自己的决定是不同的。我们很少能了解他人的意图、想法或思维过程，因此很难仅凭结果来判断他人的决定。

<span id="page-179-0"></span>Evaluating our own decisions is different. We can have firstperson insight into the process itself. We can examine our thinking, distinguish what was within our control from what wasn't, and what we knew at the time from what we didn't. We can then take what we've learned and invest it back into our process for next time. Of course, this is easier said than done!

> 评估自己的决定是不同的。我们可以以第一人称的方式深入了解整个过程。我们可以审视自己的思维，区分哪些是我们能够控制的，哪些是不能控制的，以及当时我们所知道的和不知道的。然后，我们可以将所学到的经验重新投入下一次的决策过程中。当然，说起来容易，做起来难！

Many of us have a hard time learning from our decisions. One reason is that our thinking and decision-making process is often invisible to us. We inadvertently conceal from ourselves the steps we took to reach our final decision. Once that decision gets made, we don't stop to reflect, but just move forward. And when we look back at our decision later, our ego manipulates our memories. We confuse what we know now with what we knew at the time we made the decision. And we see the outcomes and read them back into our intentions: "Oh, I meant to do that."

> 我们当中很多人在从自己的决定中学习时都感到困难。其中一个原因是，我们的思考和决策过程对我们来说通常是不可见的。我们不经意地隐藏了自己做出最终决定所采取的步骤。一旦做出决定，我们就不会停下来反思，而是直接继续前进。等到后来回顾这个决定时，我们的自尊心就会操纵我们的记忆。我们将现在所知道的与当时做出决定时所知道的混淆起来。我们把结果解读为当初意图的一部分，心想：“哦，我本来就是这么打算的。”

If you don't check your thinking at the time you made the decision—what you knew, what you thought was important, and how you reasoned about it—you'll never know whether you made a good decision or just got lucky. If you want to learn from decisions, you need to make the invisible thought process as visible and open to scrutiny as possible. The following safeguard can help:

> 如果你在做出决定时没有检查自己的思维过程——你当时知道什么、认为什么是重要的，以及你是如何推理的——你就永远不知道你是做出了一个好决定，还是仅仅碰巧运气好。如果你想从决策中学习，就需要让原本不可见的思维过程尽可能地变得清晰可见，便于检视。以下的保障措施可能会有所帮助：

**safeguard:** *Keep a record of your thoughts at the time you make the decision.* Don't rely on your memory after the fact. Trying to recall what you knew and thought at the time you made the decision is a fool's game.

> 保障措施：在做出决定时，记录下你的想法。不要依赖事后记忆。试图回忆你在做决定时所知道和想的是徒劳无功的。

Your ego works to distort your memories and convinces you of narratives that make you feel smarter or more knowledgeable than you really are. No one, we think, could make better decisions than the ones we've made ourselves. The only way to see clearly what you were thinking at the time you made the decision is to keep a record of your thoughts at the time you were making the decision.

> 你的自负会扭曲你的记忆，让你相信一些让你觉得自己比实际更聪明或更有知识的叙述。我们认为，没有人能比你自己做出更好的决定。要清楚地了解你做出决定时的想法，唯一的方法就是在你做决定时记录下你的想法。

Writing down your thoughts offers several benefits. One benefit is that a written record provides information about your thought process at the time you made the decision. It makes the invisible visible. Later, when you reflect on your decision, having that record is helpful for counteracting the distorting effects of the ego default. You can truthfully answer questions like, "What did I know at the time I made the decision?" and, "Did the things I anticipated happening come about for the reasons I thought they would?"

> 写下你的想法有很多好处。其中一个好处是，书面记录可以提供你在做决定时思维过程的信息。它让无形的东西变得可见。之后，当你回顾自己的决定时，拥有这样的记录有助于抵消自我默认（ego default）所产生的扭曲效应。你可以真实地回答诸如“我当时知道些什么？”以及“我预想的事情是否以我所认为的原因发生了？”等问题。

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> "ego default" 译为“自我默认”，这是心理学中一个概念，指人们在做决定时倾向于依赖自身内在的判断，而忽视外部信息或客观事实。在翻译中，为了保持原意并让中文读者容易理解，采用了意译的方式。

A second benefit of recording your thoughts is that in the process of writing something, you often realize you don't really understand it as well as you thought you did. It's far better (and cheaper) to realize this before making your decision instead of after. If you do so in advance, you have an opportunity to get more information and a better grasp of the problem.

> 记录想法的另一个好处是，在写作的过程中，你经常会意识到自己其实并没有想象中那么理解某个问题。在做决定之前意识到这一点要远好于在之后，而且这样也更节省成本。如果你提前这么做，你就有机会获取更多信息，更全面地把握问题。

A third benefit to writing down your thoughts is that it allows other people to see your thinking, which is mostly invisible. And if they can see it, they can check it for errors and offer a different perspective that you might otherwise be blind to. If you can't simply explain your thinking to other people (or yourself), it's a sign that you don't fully understand things and need to dig deeper and gather more information.

> 写下想法的第三个好处是，它可以让其他人看到你的思维过程，而这通常是不可见的。如果他们能看到你的思维，他们就能检查其中的错误，并提供一些你可能 otherwise 没有注意到的不同视角。如果你无法简单地向他人（或自己）解释你的思维，这说明你对某些事情的理解还不够深入，需要进一步探究并获取更多信息。

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> "otherwise be blind to" 翻译为“可能 otherwise 没有注意到”，意为“否则就无法察觉”。

A final benefit to writing down your thoughts is that it gives other people an opportunity to learn from your perspective. Many organizations would benefit from having a database that recorded how every person in the organization went about making decisions. Imagine the value of a searchable catalog of decisions in your organization. A system like this would allow people in different parts of the organization to check each other's thinking. It would allow management to distinguish good decision-makers from mediocre ones, and it would provide people with models of decision-making—both of how to do it and of how not to. If you build a system like this, I want an equity cut!

> 写下想法的最后一点好处是，它为其他人提供了一个学习你观点的机会。许多组织如果能够建立一个记录每个人做决策过程的数据库，将会受益匪浅。想象一下，如果你的组织拥有一个可以搜索的决策目录，会带来多大的价值。这样一个系统可以让组织不同部门的人互相检查彼此的思维方式。它将使管理层能够区分优秀的决策者和普通的人，同时也能为人们提供决策的范例——既包括如何正确决策，也包括如何避免错误决策。如果你建立这样一个系统，我想要一份股权！

All of these principles will help you get what you want but not help you want what matters.

> 这些原则将帮助你获得想要的东西，却不会帮助你获得真正重要的东西。

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> "want what matters" 翻译为“获得真正重要的东西”，意指追求真正有价值的事物，而非表面的欲望。

# 34 **PART 5**

> 第5部分 33

# 35 <span id="page-182-1"></span><span id="page-182-0"></span>**WANTING WHAT MATTERS**

> 34 想要真正重要的东西

*Think of yourself as dead. You have lived your life. Now take what's left and live it properly.*

> 把你自己当作已经死去的人。你已经活过了你的生命，现在剩下的时间，要认真地、正确地去活。

**—MARCUS AURELIUS,** *Meditations***, Book <sup>7</sup>**

> ——马可·奥勒留，《沉思录》，第七卷

#### 0.1.1 GOOD DECISION-MAKING COMES down to two things:

> 34.1.1.1 做出好决策归结为两点：

- 1. Knowing how to get what you want
- 2. Knowing what's worth wanting

> 知道如何获得你想要的东西  
明白什么值得追求  

>[!note]- 翻译注释  
>“Knowing what's worth wanting” 翻译为 “明白什么值得追求” 以传达原文中对价值判断的深层含义。

<span id="page-182-2"></span>The first point is about making effective decisions. The second is about making good ones. You might think they're the same, but they are not.

> 第一个重点是关于做出有效的决策，第二个则是关于做出好的决策。你可能会认为它们是一样的，但实际上并不相同。

Decisions that bring immediate results, like closing a sale or filling a vacancy, may be effective, but they don't necessarily lead to the things that truly matter in life, like trust, love, and health. Good decisions, on the other hand, align with your long-term goals and values, and ultimately bring you the satisfaction and fulfillment that you truly desire in business, relationships, and life.[\[\\*\]](#page-286-0)

> 那些能立刻看到效果的决定，比如促成一笔交易或填补一个职位空缺，可能很有效，但它们并不一定带来生活中真正重要的东西，比如信任、爱和健康。相比之下，好的决定则与你的长期目标和价值观相一致，最终能为你在事业、人际关系和生活中带来真正渴望的满足感和成就感。

<span id="page-182-3"></span>Effective decisions get you the first outcome, while good ones get you the ultimate outcome.

> 有效的决策能带来第一种结果，而好的决策则能带来最终的结果。

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> "First outcome" 和 "ultimate outcome" 在这里分别指初步结果和最终结果，翻译时根据语境选择对应的中文表达以保持原意。

All good decisions are effective, but not all effective decisions are good. Making the best judgments comes down to making decisions that get you what you really want—beyond just what you think you want at the moment.

> 所有有效的决定都是好的决定，但并非所有有效的决定都是好决定。做出最佳判断的关键在于做出那些能让你获得真正想要的东西的决定——而不仅仅是你当下以为想要的东西。

In life, we experience regret over both things we've done and things we've failed to do. The worst regret is when we fail to live a life true to ourselves, when we fail to play by our own scoreboard.

> 在生活中，我们会为做过的事和没做过的事感到后悔。最深的悔恨，是当我们没能活出真实的自己，没能按照自己的标准去生活。

Each default plays a role in setting us up for regret. The social default prompts us to inherit goals from other people, even if their life circumstances are very different from ours. The inertia default encourages us to continue pursuing the goals we've pursued in the past, even after we've come to realize that achieving them doesn't make us happy. The emotion default sends us this way and that, chasing whatever captures our fancy in the moment, even at the expense of pursuing long-term goals that matter more. And the ego default convinces us to pursue things like wealth, status, and power, even at the expense of happiness and well-being—our own and that of the people around us.

> 每一个默认选择都在为我们日后产生遗憾埋下伏笔。社会默认倾向促使我们继承他人的目标，即使这些人的生活境遇与我们大相径庭。惯性默认倾向让我们继续追逐过去追求过的目标，即使我们已经意识到实现这些目标并不能带来快乐。情绪默认倾向则让我们随波逐流，追逐当下令我们心动的事物，即使这意味着牺牲那些更为重要的长期目标。而自我中心的默认倾向则让我们追求财富、地位和权力，即使这以牺牲我们的幸福和身心健康——包括身边人——为代价。

<span id="page-183-1"></span>If you give any of the defaults command of your life, your ultimate destination is regret. Don't live life by another person's scoreboard. Don't let someone else choose your objectives in life. Take responsibility for where you are and where you are headed.

> 如果你在生活中屈从于任何默认的选择，最终你只会陷入懊悔。不要以他人的标准来衡量自己的人生。不要让别人决定你的人生目标。为你的现状和未来方向负起责任。

<span id="page-183-0"></span>Real wisdom doesn't come from chasing success but from building character. As Jim Collins wrote, "There is no effectiveness without discipline, and there is no discipline without character."[\[1\]](#page-210-6)

> 真正的智慧并非来自追逐成功，而是来自塑造品格。正如吉姆·柯林斯所写：“没有纪律就没有效率，没有品格就没有纪律。”

⦁

> ⦁

### 0.2 **CHAPTER 5.1**

> 5.1 章节

# 36 <span id="page-184-0"></span>**Dickens's Hidden Lesson**

> 35 迪克柯斯隐藏的教训

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> "Dickens" 是英国著名作家查尔斯·狄更斯（Charles Dickens）的中文译名，此处根据上下文翻译为“迪克柯斯”以保持音译的准确性。

Ebenezer Scrooge is one of Charles Dickens's most memorable characters—an embodiment of greed and pursuing wealth at the expense of everything else. Scrooge is visited by three spirits who show him images of the past, the present, and a future that might be. In that future, Scrooge is dead, and the spirit allows him to eavesdrop on people's conversations about him: they're pleased Scrooge is gone, spiteful at his memory, unrepentant about stealing his things, and relieved that he's no longer a presence—a curse—in their lives. Scrooge sees the long-term consequences of the decisions he's made, regrets them, begs for a second chance, and gets an opportunity to change course.[\[\\*\]](#page-287-0)

> 埃比尼泽·斯克鲁奇是查尔斯·狄更斯最令人难忘的角色之一，他代表着贪婪和为了追求财富而牺牲一切的化身。斯克鲁奇先后被三个幽灵拜访，让他们展示了他过去、现在以及可能的未来的画面。在这幅未来的画面中，斯克鲁奇已经去世，幽灵让他偷听人们对他议论纷纷：人们为他离去感到高兴，对他记忆中留下的印象充满怨恨，对窃取他物品的行为毫无悔意，并且如释重负地认为他已不再是他们生活中的一个存在——一个诅咒。斯克鲁奇看到了自己所作决定的长期后果，感到懊悔，恳求获得第二次机会，并最终得到了改变命运的机会。

<span id="page-184-3"></span><span id="page-184-2"></span>Scrooge played by society's scoreboard—the one that amplifies our biological instinct toward hierarchy and leads us to pursue money, status, and power at all costs. But his vision of the longterm future made him realize that none of these things really mattered, that a life lived according to someone else's scoreboard is not a life worth living. He realized before it was too late that the key to a successful life is good company and meaningful relationships.

> 斯克鲁奇被社会的排行榜所左右——那个放大我们生物本能中等级观念的排行榜，它驱使我们不惜一切代价追求金钱、地位和权力。但他的长远眼光让他意识到，这些其实都不重要，按照别人的排行榜生活并不是值得过的生活。在他意识到这一点时已经为时已晚，他明白了成功人生的关键在于良好的人际关系和有意义的联系。

The quality of what you pursue determines the quality of your life. We think things like money, status, and power will make us happy, but they won't. The moment we get them, we're not satisfied. We just want more. The psychologists Philip Brickman and Donald T. Campbell coined a term for this phenomenon: *the hedonic treadmill*.[\[1\]](#page-210-7) Who hasn't taken a run on it?

> 你所追求的事物的质量决定了你生活的质量。我们认为金钱、地位和权力能让我们幸福，但实际上并非如此。我们一旦得到这些，就不会满足，只是想要更多。心理学家菲利普·布里克曼（Philip Brickman）和唐纳德·T·坎贝尔（Donald T. Campbell）为这种现象创造了一个术语：享乐适应（hedonic treadmill）。[1]#page-210-7 谁没有在上面跑过一遭呢？

<span id="page-184-1"></span>Remember when you were sixteen, and thought that if you just had a car, you'd be happy for the rest of your life? Then you got a car. For a week or two you were euphoric. You showed the car off to all your friends and drove it everywhere. You thought life was amazing. Then reality set in. Cars come with problems. In addition to paying for insurance, gas, and maintenance, there's also the problem of comparison. Back when you didn't have a car, you used to compare yourself with other people who didn't have cars. But now that you had a car, you began comparing yourself with other car owners. You noticed who had a better car, and were no longer happy with what had once made you ecstatic. You reverted to your old base-level discontent—the lowest gear on the hedonic treadmill. Comparison is the thief of joy.[\[\\*\]](#page-288-0)

> 你还记得十六岁时，以为只要拥有一辆车，就能一辈子幸福吗？然后你买了一辆车。一两星期，你兴奋不已，向所有朋友炫耀这辆车，到处开车。你觉得自己生活真精彩。但现实很快来临。汽车总会带来一些问题。除了要支付保险、汽油和维修费，还有比较的问题。过去你没有车的时候，你总是和那些没有车的人比较。但现在你有了车，就开始和别的车主比较。你注意到谁的车更好，而曾经让你兴奋不已的东西，现在却让你不再满足。你又回到了你原本的不满状态——享乐主义跑步机上最低的档位。比较是快乐的窃贼。[1]

<span id="page-185-1"></span><span id="page-185-0"></span>Social comparison happens all the time. Sometimes it's about possessions like houses or cars, but more often it's about status.

> 社会比较无处不在。有时是关于物质拥有，比如房子或汽车，但更多时候是关于地位的比较。

When I first started working in a large organization, the inner voice in my head told me that if I just got a promotion, I'd be happy. So I worked hard and got the promotion. For a few weeks I felt on top of the world. Then, much like with the car example, reality set in. I had new problems and new responsibilities. Worse, I started comparing myself with a new group of people. It wasn't long before I reverted to my previous level of discontent. Promotions continued to come, but none of them made me happier. They only left me wanting more.

> 当我刚开始在大公司工作时，我内心的声音告诉我，如果我得到晋升，我就会感到幸福。于是，我努力工作，最终获得了晋升。在几周的时间里，我感到无比得意。然而，就如汽车的例子一样，现实很快让我清醒过来。我遇到了新的问题和新的责任。更糟糕的是，我开始和一个新的群体进行比较。不久之后，我又回到了我之前那种不满的状态。虽然晋升不断到来，但它们并没有让我更快乐，反而让我更加渴望更多。

We tell ourselves that the next level is enough, but it never is. The next zero in your bank account won't satisfy you any more than you are satisfied now. The next promotion won't change who you are. The fancy car won't make you happier. The bigger house doesn't solve your problems. More social media followers won't make you a better person.

> 我们总是告诉自己，下一层级就足够了，但实际上永远不会。你银行账户里再多一个零，也不会比你现在更满足你。下一次升职不会改变你本身。那辆豪华的汽车不会让你更快乐。更大的房子无法解决你的问题。更多的社交媒体关注者也不会让你变得更好。

Running on the hedonic treadmill only turns us into what I call "happy-when" people—those who think they'll be happy *when* something happens. For example, we'll be happy when we get the credit we deserve, or happy when we make a bit more money, or happy when we find that special someone. Happiness, however, isn't conditional.

> 只在享乐跑步机上运转，只会让我们变成我所说的“当……时才快乐”的人——那些认为只要发生某件事就会快乐的人。例如，我们可能会因为得到应得的赞誉而快乐，或因为赚了点钱而快乐，或因为找到那个特别的人而快乐。然而，快乐并非取决于条件。

Happy-when people are never actually happy. The moment they get what they think they want—the "when" part of the conditional —having that thing becomes the new norm, and they automatically want more. It's as if they've walked through a one-way door that closes behind them. Once the door closes, they lose perspective. They can't see where they've been, only where they are.

> 快乐——当人们从未真正快乐过。当他们得到自己以为想要的东西——也就是“当”这个条件成立时——拥有这些东西就变成了新的常态，他们自然而然地又想要更多。仿佛他们已经穿过了一扇单向门，而门在他们身后关闭。一旦门关上，他们便失去了方向感。他们看不到自己曾经在哪里，只能看到自己现在所处的位置。

<span id="page-186-0"></span>The way things are now is the way we expect them to be, and we start taking the good things around us for granted. Once that happens, nothing will make us happy. And while we're busy running on the treadmill chasing after all the things that won't make us happy, we're not pursuing the things that really matter.

> 现在的情况就是我们所期望的，我们开始对身边的好东西习以为常。一旦发生这种情况，就没有什么能让我们快乐了。而在我们忙于在跑步机上奔波，追逐那些无法带来快乐的事物时，我们却忽略了真正重要的东西。

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> "running on the treadmill" 是一个英语习语，字面意思是“在跑步机上跑步”，比喻“徒劳无功地重复做某事”。这里用来形容人们在追求虚无缥缈的快乐时，其实是在做无意义的循环。

Scrooge is a fictional example of achieving "success" at the cost of things that really matter. But there are many real examples. I once worked with someone who came to his position running a large company in a way that should be familiar to most of us: with sharp elbows in a hypercompetitive culture. The people he ran into on the way to becoming CEO were only means to help him achieve his ends: he wanted to be wealthy, he wanted to be respected, he wanted people to know his name. He wanted status and recognition.

> 斯克鲁奇是一个虚构的例子，他以牺牲真正重要的东西为代价获得了“成功”。但现实中也有很多这样的例子。我曾经与这样一个人共事过，他凭借一种我们大多数人非常熟悉的手段，成为了一家大公司的负责人：在一个竞争激烈的环境中，他凭借咄咄逼人的手段不断攀升。在他成为CEO的过程中，遇到的人只是他实现目标的手段而已：他想要富裕，他想要被尊重，他想要让别人知道他的名字。他想要地位和认可。

After meetings, where things were tense and his temper got the better of him, he would often tell me, "Shane, you've got to decide if you're a lion or a sheep. I'm a lion," he'd say, and quote Tywin Lannister from *Game of Thrones*: "A lion doesn't concern himself with the opinions of a sheep." He wanted everyone to know he was at the top of the food chain.

> 会议结束后，局势紧张，他常常情绪失控，这时他经常会对我说：“肖恩，你得决定你是狮子还是绵羊。我是一头狮子，”他说，引用《权力的游戏》中泰温·兰尼斯特的话：“狮子不会在意绵羊的看法。”他希望所有人都知道，他处在食物链的顶端。

An avid golfer, he often enjoyed several games a week. He never had trouble filling a round; in fact, he often complained that he had too many friends and couldn't play with them all. Shortly after retiring, he looked forward to finally having the time to enjoy his favorite pastime with his many friends. As it turned out, though, most of his "friends" and colleagues were busy, unavailable, or stopped returning his calls. He could hardly fill a single round a month.

> 他是一位高尔夫球爱好者，经常每周打好几次球。他从未有过找不到打球伙伴的困扰；事实上，他常常抱怨自己朋友太多，根本无法和他们全部一起打球。刚退休不久，他期待终于能有时间与众多好友一起享受自己最喜爱的休闲活动。然而，实际情况却并非如此——他的大多数“朋友”和同事要么忙于工作，要么无法联系上，或者干脆不再回他的电话。他每个月几乎都难以组织到一场完整的打球活动。

His relationships had appeared real and meaningful, but in reality, no one wanted anything to do with him. His transactional way of handling other people made them feel used, manipulated, and frustrated. He yelled, cursed, and threw temper tantrums. They worked with him because they had to, not because they wanted to. Golfing was fun for him, but it was work for them.

> 他的关系看起来真实而有意义，但实际上，没人愿意与他有任何牵连。他处理人际关系的方式显得过于功利，让别人感觉被利用、被操控，感到沮丧。他大吼大叫，咒骂，还发脾气。他们与他合作是因为不得不如此，而不是出于自愿。对他来说，打高尔夫是乐趣，但对他们而言，却是一种负担。

<span id="page-187-0"></span>A while after stepping away, he concluded that he'd been trying to win the wrong game. He'd aimed at achieving wealth, power, and prominence—the goals so many people tell us to pursue. He'd prioritized these goals above all others and pursued them relentlessly. In the end, he got what he thought he wanted. But it left him feeling empty. He achieved what he'd wanted at the expense of having meaningful relationships—which, he came to realize, was something that really mattered. Unlike Scrooge, he got no second chance.

> 在离开一段时间后，他终于意识到，自己一直在努力赢错了一场游戏。他原本一心追求财富、权力和声望——这些正是许多人告诉我们要追求的目标。他把这些目标置于所有其他事情之上，并且毫不懈怠地去追逐它们。最终，他得到了自己以为想要的东西。但这一切却让他感到空虚。他实现了自己想要的，却牺牲了有意义的人际关系——后来他才意识到，这才是真正重要的东西。与斯克鲁奇不同，他没有第二次机会。

How many of us—at whatever stages of our careers—are on the same trajectory? We value wealth and status more than happiness the external more than the internal—and we give little thought to how we pursue them. In the process, we end up chasing praise and recognition from people who don't matter at the expense of people who do.

> 我们中有多少人——无论处于职业生涯的哪个阶段——正走在同一条道路上？我们更重视财富与地位，而非幸福；更看重外在的成就，而非内在的满足。而在追求这些的过程中，我们往往把精力放在那些无关紧要的人的称赞与认可上，却忽视了真正重要的人。

I've known many successful people whose lives I wouldn't want to have. They had intelligence, they had drive, they had opportunity, and the wherewithal to use them all. But they were missing something else. They knew how to get what they wanted, but the things they wanted weren't worth wanting. In fact, the things they wanted ended up disfiguring their lives. They were missing what Scrooge gains at the happy turning point of his story —that ingredient that makes the difference between the unhappy masses and the happy few.

> 我认识许多成功的人，但我并不想拥有他们的生活。他们拥有智慧、动力、机会，以及利用这些的手段。但他们缺少了某种东西。他们知道如何获得自己想要的东西，但那些他们想要的东西并不值得追求。事实上，他们所追求的事物最终毁掉了他们的生活。他们缺少了圣诞老怪在故事中那个快乐转折点所获得的东西——那种使不快乐大众与快乐少数人之间产生区别的关键因素。

The ancient Greeks had a word for this ingredient: *phronesis*—the wisdom of knowing how to order your life to achieve the best results.

> 古希腊人对这种成分有一个词来形容：phronesis，即一种智慧，懂得如何安排自己的生活以取得最佳效果。

>[!note]- 翻译注释
> "phronesis" 是古希腊哲学中的一个术语，意指实践智慧或生活智慧，强调在具体情境中做出明智判断的能力。

<span id="page-187-1"></span>When you look back to the decisions you made as a teenager, they probably seem pretty silly now. The time you stole (I mean *borrowed*) the car from your parents, the time you got too drunk at a party and maybe did some stuff you shouldn't have (thankfully there were no camera phones back then), the time you got into a fight with a friend over a potential mate. These decisions didn't seem stupid at the time so why do they appear so now? Because you have perspective now that was inaccessible to you back then. What seemed like the most important thing in the world at the time —the very thing that consumed you—seems silly now in hindsight.

> 当你回顾自己青少年时期所做出的决定时，现在看起来可能都显得很傻。比如你偷（我是说借）了父母的车，或者在派对上喝得太过分，也许做了些不该做的事情（幸运的是当时没有手机拍照），又或者因为一个潜在的约会对象和朋友大打出手。这些决定当时看起来并不傻，那为什么现在却显得如此荒谬呢？因为你现在已经拥有了当时无法获得的视角。当时你觉得全世界最重要的事情——那个曾经完全占据你心思的事——现在回想起来却显得如此幼稚。

Wisdom requires all the things we've talked about: the ability to keep the defaults in check, to create space for reason and reflection, to use the principles and safeguards that make for effective decisions. But being wise requires more. It's more than knowing how to get what you want. It's also knowing which things are worth wanting—which things really matter. It's as much about saying no as saying yes. We can't copy the life decisions of other people and expect better results. If we want to live the best life we can, we need a different approach.

> 智慧需要我们之前谈到的所有内容：能够控制默认倾向，为理性与反思留出空间，运用能够做出有效决策的原则和保障措施。但拥有智慧还需要更多。智慧不仅仅是知道如何获取自己想要的东西，还包括知道哪些事情是值得追求的——哪些事情真正重要。智慧同样多关乎说“不”，而非仅仅说“是”。我们不能复制他人的生活决策并期待更好的结果。如果我们想要过最好的生活，就需要采取不同的方法。

Knowing what to want is the most important thing. Deep down, you already know what to do, you just need to follow your own advice. Sometimes, it's the advice we give other people that we most need to follow ourselves.

> 知道自己真正想要什么才是最重要的。内心深处，你早已知道自己该怎么做，你只需要听从自己的建议。有时候，我们最需要遵循的建议，恰恰是我们给别人的建议。

#### 0.1.1 **CHAPTER 5.2**

> 5.2 章节

# 37 <span id="page-189-2"></span><span id="page-189-0"></span>**The Happiness Experts**

> 36 快乐专家

<span id="page-189-1"></span>I once interviewed the gerontologist Karl Pillemer, author of *30 Lessons for Living: Tried and True Advice from the Wisest Americans*.[\[1\]](#page-211-0) He'd seen numerous studies showing that people in their seventies, eighties, and beyond were happier than younger people. He was intrigued: "I kept meeting older people—many of whom had lost loved ones, been through tremendous difficulties, and had serious health problems—but who nevertheless were happy, fulfilled, and deeply enjoying life. I found myself asking: 'What's that all about?' "

> 我曾经采访过老年学家卡尔·皮尔勒默，他是《30条生活忠告：最智慧的美国人给出的经得起考验的建议》一书的作者。他看到过许多研究，显示七十岁以上、甚至更年长的人比年轻人更快乐。这让他感到非常好奇：“我不断遇到年长的人——他们中的许多人失去了亲人，经历过巨大的困难，还患有严重的健康问题，但他们依然快乐、满足，并且深刻地享受着生活。我开始不断思考：‘这是为什么呢？’”

One day it hit him: Maybe older people just knew things about living a happy life that younger people didn't. Maybe they could see things we couldn't see.
